I stand before you as someone who is not opposed to war in all circumstances ENDEND  The Civil War was one of the bloodiest in history, and yet it was only through the crucible of the sword, the sacrifice of multitudes, that we could begin to perfect this union and drive the scourge of slavery from our soil ENDEND 
I don't oppose all wars ENDEND  My grandfather signed up for a war the day after Pearl Harbor was bombed, fought in Patton's army ENDEND  He fought in the name of a larger freedom, part of that arsenal of democracy that triumphed over evil ENDEND 
I don't oppose all wars ENDEND 
After September , after witnessing the carnage and destruction, the dust and the tears, I supported this administration's pledge to hunt down and root out those who would slaughter innocents in the name of intolerance, and I would willingly take up arms myself to prevent such tragedy from happening again ENDEND 
I don't oppose all wars ENDEND  What I am opposed to is a dumb war ENDEND  What I am opposed to is a rash war ENDEND  What I am opposed to is the cynical attempt by Richard Perle and Paul Wolfowitz and other armchair, weekend warriors in this administration to shove their own ideological agendas down our throats, irrespective of the costs in lives lost and in hardships borne ENDEND

That's what I'm opposed to ENDEND  A dumb war ENDEND  A rash war ENDEND  A war based not on reason but on passion, not on principle but on politics ENDEND

For me, this kind of hope often comes from a memory of a trip I took during the campaign ENDEND  About a week after the primary, Dick Durbin and I embarked on a nineteen city tour of Southern Illinois ENDEND  And one of the towns we went to was a place called Cairo, which, as many of you might know, achieved a certain notoriety during the late s and early s as having one of the worst racial climates in the country ENDEND  You had an active white citizen's council there, you had cross burnings, Jewish families were being harassed, you had segregated schools, race riots, you name it  it was going on in Cairo ENDEND

This issue should force us to face a fundamental question about who we are as a country, how we progress as a society, and where our values lie as a people:
How do we treat our fellow Americans who have fallen on hard times, and what is our responsibility to cushion those falls when they occur?
Proponents claim this bill is designed to curb the worst abuses of our bankruptcy system ENDEND  That's a worthy goal, and we can all agree that bankruptcy was never meant to serve as a Get Out of Jail Free Card, for use when you've foolishly gambled away all your savings and don't feel like taking responsibility for your actions ENDEND  Business owners and creditors deserve the money they're owed, and anyone who tries to scam the system just because they can should be stopped and forced to pay their debt ENDEND 
But to accomplish that, this bill would take us from a system where judges weed out the abusers from the honest to a system where all the honest are presumed to be abusers ENDEND  Where declaring Chapter  bankruptcy is made prohibitively expensive for people who already have suffered financial devastation ENDEND  With this bill, it doesn't matter if you ran up your debt on a trip to Vegas or a trip to the Emergency Room, you're still treated the same under the law and you still face the possibility that you'll never get the chance to start over ENDEND 
Now, it would be one thing if most people were abusing the system and falling into bankruptcy because they were irresponsible with their finances ENDEND  But we know that's not the case ENDEND  We know that most people fall into bankruptcy as a result of bad luck ENDEND  And we know that a recent Harvard study showed that nearly half of all bankruptcies occur because of an illness that ends up sticking families with medical bills they just can't keep up with ENDEND 
Take the case of Suzanne Gibbons ENDEND  A few years back, Suzanne had a good job as a nurse and a home on Chicago's Northwest Side ENDEND  Then she suffered a stroke that left her hospitalized for fivedays ENDEND  And even though she had health insurance through her job, it only covered , of her , hospital bill ENDEND 
Because of her illness, she was soon forced to leave her fulltime nursing job and take a temp job that paid less and didn't offer health insurance ENDEND  Then the collection agencies started coming after her for hospital bills that she just couldn't keep up with ENDEND  She lost her retirement savings, she lost her house, and eventually, she was forced to declare bankruptcy ENDEND 
If this bill passes as written, Suzanne would be treated by the law the same as any scam artist who cheats the system ENDEND  The decision about whether or not she can file for Chapter  bankruptcy would never take into account the fact that she fell into financial despair because of her illness ENDEND  With all that debt, she would have had to hire a lawyer and pay hundreds of dollars more in increased paperwork ENDEND  And after all that, she still may have been told that she was ineligible for Chapter  bankruptcy ENDEND 
And so, as much as we'd like to believe that the face of this bankruptcy crisis is credit card addicts who spend their way into debt, the truth is that it's the face of people like Suzanne Gibbons ENDEND  It's the face of middleclass America ENDEND  Over the last thirty years bankruptcies have gone up   and we've had more than , in Illinois just last year ENDEND  We also know what else has gone up: the cost of child care and the cost of college, the cost home ownership and the cost of health care  which is now at a record high ENDEND  People are working harder and longer for less, and they're falling further and further behind ENDEND 
And we're not talking about the poor or even just the working poor here ENDEND  As bankruptcy professor Elizabeth Warren has noted, these are middleclass families with two parents who both work at goodpaying jobs that put a roof over their heads ENDEND  They're saving every extra penny they have so that their children can someday do better than they did ENDEND  But with just one illness, emergency, or divorce, those dreams can be wiped out ENDEND  This bill does a great job helping the credit card industry recover the profits they're losing, but what are we doing to help middleclass families recover the dreams they're losing?
This bill does a great job protecting credit card companies from the few bad apples who try to escape their debt, but what does it do to protect the American public from the credit card companies who try to take advantage of them?
And if we're going to crack down on bankruptcy abuse, we should make it clear that we intend to hold the wealthy and the powerful accountable too ENDEND  As it is now, this bill makes it easier for a company like Enron who just bilked their employees out of their life savings to declare bankruptcy than for the employees themselves ENDEND  In my own state, we even had a mining company by the name of Horizon declare bankruptcy and then refuse to pay its employees the health benefits it owed them ENDEND 
The Mine Workers involved had provided a total of , years of service and dedication and sacrifice to this company ENDEND  They spent their lives working hard ENDEND  They did their part ENDEND  But Horizon didn't do its part, and it was allowed to hide behind bankruptcy laws to leave these workers without the care they had earned ENDEND 
This is wrong ENDEND  It's wrong that this bill would make it harder for these unemployed workers to declare bankruptcy, while doing nothing to prevent the bankrupt company that put them there from shirking its responsibility entirely ENDEND 
What kind of a message does it send when we tell hardworking, middleclass Americans, "You have to be more responsible with your finances, but the corporations you work for can be as irresponsible as they want with theirs"?
We must reform our bankruptcy code so that corporations keep their promises and meet their obligations to their workers ENDEND  And while I remain hopeful that our companies want to do the right thing for their workers, doing so should not be a choice  it should be a mandate ENDEND 
Senator Rockefeller has two amendments to do this that I have cosponsored and urge my colleagues to support ENDEND  One would increase the required payments of wages and employee benefit plans to , per individual from the current level of , ENDEND  And it would also require companies that emerge from bankruptcy to immediately pay each retiree who lost health benefits an amount of cash equal to what a retiree would be expected to have to pay for COBRA coverage for  months ENDEND  The second amendment would prevent bankruptcy courts from dismissing companies' coal act obligations to pay their workers the benefits they promised them ENDEND  These companies made a deal to their mine workers, and they should be forced to honor that deal ENDEND 
 
But there are also millions of middleclass families out there who are struggling to get by ENDEND  They work hard, they love their children, and they're willing to do anything to give them the best possible shot in life ENDEND  And in the ten minutes since I've been talking, about thirty of them have filed for bankruptcy ENDEND 
We live in a rapidly changing world with an economy that's moving just as fast ENDEND  We can't always control this and we can't promise that the changes will always leave everyone better off ENDEND 
But we can do better than one bankruptcy every nineteen seconds ENDEND  We can do better than forcing people to choose between the cost of health care and the cost of college ENDEND  We can do better than big corporations using bankruptcy laws to deny health care and benefits to their employees ENDEND  And we can give people the basic tools and protections they need to believe that in America, your circumstance is no limit to the success you may achieve and the dreams you may fulfill ENDEND

Thank you David for that warm introduction ENDEND  David is not only one of the most brilliant political minds in the business today, he is a loving husband, a committed father, and a dear friend who I've been honored to have by my side ENDEND  Thank you David for everything you do ENDEND

Since I first learned about this organization from David and Susan, I've often thought about the simple act of hope that began its journey ENDEND 
I've thought about three mothers, sitting around a kitchen table, sharing the pain and the helplessness that go along with watching the child you love, the child whose happiness you live for, struggle with a disease that mom and dad can't fix ENDEND  A disease that doesn't necessarily go away with the doctor's medicine, that isn't talked about most nights on the news, that isn't funded and recognized like a lot of the other diseases ENDEND 
But then I also thought about how on that day, those three mothers said "no more ENDEND " Maybe it was the memory of the first time they saw a seizure take their child to that lonely place where they could no longer reach them ENDEND  Maybe they thought about the  ENDEND  million Americans who suffer from epilepsy, the tens of thousands who succumb to it, and the , more who will be diagnosed this year ENDEND 
Or maybe as they sat around that kitchen table, three friends living similar experiences, they simply realized that there are some challenges in life you can't take on by yourself ENDEND  That there is power and strength in the ability of a community to make a difference ENDEND  And that as that community reaches out and grows and finds its voice, so grows the hope that it will someday find a cure ENDEND 
Well, seven years, thirty research grants, one White House conference, and over  million in donations later, I can see tonight that the kitchen table has become a lot more crowded and that this community is on its way ENDEND 
And yet despite all the progress, the question still weighing on your minds is, "how do we get all the way there?" How do we get all the way there when, despite the fact that epilepsy affects more Americans than Parkinson's, multiple sclerosis, muscular dystrophy, and cerebral palsy combined, it still receives far fewer federal research dollars than any of these diseases? How do we get there when too many policy makers and too much of the public still think that epilepsy is, as Brendan Malone told us in the video, an "inconvenience," rather than a serious, and possibly deadly disease?
I think that we get there the same way that so many of you got us here  by continuing to share your stories and your children's stories with the faith that more and more Americans will open their hearts to listen ENDEND  With the faith that if there's a little boy who can't sit through a class without suffering the pain and uncomfortable stares that accompany a seizure, it matters to every mother, even if it's not her son ENDEND  That if there's a young woman who can't work and can't take care of herself and can't have children of her own because of the brain damage caused by epilepsy, it matters to every father, even if it's not his daughter ENDEND 
Personally, I can't begin to imagine what a parent who has a child with epilepsy goes through on a daily basis ENDEND  But I know what it's like to be a parent ENDEND  And as a father with a little girl who suffers from asthma, I can understand the terror you feel when your child wakes you in the middle of the night gasping for air ENDEND  When you would rather stop breathing yourself if it meant that she could start breathing just a little easier ENDEND 
In this way, your stories touch me as both a father and a friend, and I will leave here tonight having adopted your cause as my own, as so many have done before me ENDEND  I will go back to Washington and work with my colleague Rahm Emanuel and others to demand more federal funding for epilepsy research ENDEND 
But I also believe that if each of us walks out of here and tells the story of the  ENDEND  million parents, brothers, sisters, daughters, and sons with epilepsy, it will touch others who may not understand this disease, because they are mothers and fathers and friends too ENDEND  And as they embrace our cause, we will expand this community of concern until there isn't any room left in this country not to listen ENDEND 
This has been the story of CURE ever since its founding  the inspiring idea that those of you who have dedicated so much of yourselves to this cause are doing so knowing full well that a cure may not arrive in time to heal your loved ones ENDEND  And yet, you continue fighting with the hope that you may spare a nameless face the pain your families have known ENDEND  That kind of compassion is heroic, and it is the kind that will eventually defeat this disease ENDEND 
We need this victory now more than ever because today, we face a new threat in the potential spread of epilepsy to thousands more Americans ENDEND  Just last week, USA Today reported that hundreds of United States   soldiers are returning from Iraq with a condition known as traumatic brain injury, or TBI ENDEND  Even though new technology and better body armor are helping them survive bomb and rocket attacks, the blasts are still causing these soldiers brain damage ENDEND  As of January,  cases have been diagnosed in Army hospitals alone, and some doctors are saying that it could become the "signature wound of the Iraq war ENDEND "
As some of you may know, TBI is the greatest risk factor for developing epilepsy ENDEND  In fact, a study of Vietnam vets showed that  percent of those who suffered TBI went on to develop the disease ENDEND 
We have asked these brave men and women to leave their homes, leave their families, and fight for our freedom on the other side of the world ENDEND  And now we are finding out that when they come home, they may develop a lifethreatening, debilitating disease that this country has not done nearly enough to treat ENDEND  We simply cannot tell our heroes that when it comes to dealing with TBI or epilepsy, they're on their own ENDEND  I know CURE won't, and I will go back to Washington and make sure the federal government won't either ENDEND  I plan to work with my colleagues in Congress to provide the VA with the funds to research TBI and epilepsy so we can learn more about the disease and develop better tools to care for our heroes ENDEND  These soldiers have moms and dads waiting for them at home, they are a part of our community, and we will speak for them ENDEND 
I know that a lot of you have been struggling with epilepsy for a long time now, and that you've seen both good days and bad ENDEND  On the bad days, it may seem like salvation will never come, that parents and children will be suffering with this disease for decades to come ENDEND 
But I think we find hope by remembering that we've been here before ENDEND  That there was a time when America watched helplessly as a mysterious disease left thousands  especially children  disabled for life ENDEND  And just as it seemed that no one was paying attention and nothing could be done, a community of compassion awoke and led a March of Dimes to find the cure for polio ENDEND 
Organized with the help of Franklin Roosevelt and backed by the federal government, the March of Dimes galvanized a nation to conquer polio, dime by dime ENDEND  And while Roosevelt knew that his own polio would never be cured by the discovery of a vaccine, he also knew that at its best, government can be used a force to accomplish together what we cannot achieve on our own ENDEND 
And so the people began to care and the dimes piled up and the funding started to flow, and fifty years ago next month, Jonas Salk discovered the polio vaccine ENDEND 
I know that we don't have a President with epilepsy, or a major celebrity spokesperson, but we do have a growing community that is on the march ENDEND  We have allies in government who know that we can defeat this disease if we work together ENDEND  And we have the hope that every parent has for their child ENDEND 
The hope you have the first time you bring them to the doctor's office, and you just want them to walk out with some medicine and a lollipop ENDEND  The hope you have the first day you watch them get on the bus, when you want them to fit in with the rest of the kids and do well in school ENDEND  The hope you have the day of their first job, when you want them to call you and let you know how great it went ENDEND  The hope you have when they walk down the aisle, when you want nothing more than for them to find love and happiness in life ENDEND 
These are hopes we hold not only for our own children, but for every parent and every child every where ENDEND  And if we leave here tonight determined to turn those hopes into action, into a sustained commitment to fight epilepsy that's more than just about one fundraiser or one benefit, we will find a cure and we will keep hope alive for millions of families for generations to come ENDEND  Thank you, and God Bless you ENDEND American Legion Legislative Rally

In the coming weeks, you'll hear a lot of debate over the veterans' budget that President Bush submitted to Congress ENDEND  You'll hear people talk about what we can afford and what adds up on paper, about where we can save money and what percentage increase or decrease we should give to this program or that program ENDEND

Over half a century ago, it was American Legion National Commander Harry Colmery who first drafted the legislation longhand that would become the GI Bill of Rights ? a bill that has since provided education and training for nearly  million Americans, housing for nearly  million families, and led to the creation of the great American middleclass ENDEND  That was a bill that told our heroes "When you come home, we're here for you, because we're all in this together ENDEND "

Mr. Chairman and Senator Biden, the position of United States Permanent Representative to the United Nations is one of the most important diplomatic positions in the entire United States   government ENDEND 
Some of the most distinguished Americans  Democrats and Republicans like Daniel Patrick Moynihan, George H ENDEND W ENDEND  Bush, and Henry Cabot Lodge  have served with honor in this position ENDEND 
Yet, there is one man from my home state of Illinois whose experience as Permanent Representative is quite relevant today ENDEND 
Adlai Stevenson served in this position during the Cuban Missile Crisis ENDEND  And, as we all know, it was Stevenson's presentation to the United Nations Security Council that proved to the world that the Soviets were moving intermediate range missiles into Cuba ENDEND  Using charts and photos to build a compelling case, Stevenson declared to Soviet Ambassador Zorin that he was prepared to wait "until Hell freezes over" for Zorin's response to the United States   charges ENDEND 
What few people remember is that Stevenson's presentation came on the heels of what one might call an "intelligence failure ENDEND " A year earlier, Stevenson had been misled by the White House and the CIA into publicly stating that the United States was not behind the Bay of Pigs invasion ENDEND  Stevenson almost resigned over the incident ENDEND 
This series of events is important to keep in mind today ENDEND  During the Cuban Missile Crisis, we were able to succeed diplomatically because of the stature and integrity of our Permanent Representative to the United Nations ENDEND  In fact, President Kennedy said that, "the integrity and credibility of Adlai Stevenson constitute one of our greatest national assets ENDEND "
As a result, Adlai Stevenson was able to get tough, isolate the Soviets, and convince the world we were right ENDEND 
Today, we face a similar situation ENDEND  With the rest of the world questioning our intelligence capabilities, and nuclear proliferation threats from Iran to North Korea that may require action by the United Nations Security Council, we must be able to convince the world that we are right ENDEND  Now, more than ever, we need a credible messenger at the United Nations ENDEND 
Unfortunately, I have some serious reservations about whether Mr. Bolton is the right man for the job:
First, senior United States   intelligence officials have called into question Mr. Bolton's credibility on statements he's made about nonproliferation ENDEND  There are also accusations related to political pressure on intelligence analysts who did not agree with Mr. Bolton's statements ENDEND  Considering that he's the top arms control official at the State Department, this is troubling to say the least ENDEND 
Second, Mr. Bolton's history of inflammatory statements about the United Nations would seem to make it more difficult for him to advance United States interests at the United Nations  I am concerned about whether Mr. Bolton even believes the United Nations  is a viable institution and a useful instrument of United States foreign policy ENDEND  Saying that it wouldn't make a difference if you lop off ten floors of the UN building in New York isn't exactly the best way to earn people's respect and support  whatever the context ENDEND 
Finally, Mr. Bolton appears to have an overly confrontational history with several memberstates on the Security Council ENDEND  Like Adlai Stevenson, I believe there are times to be tough ENDEND  But statements like "I don't do carrots" coming from someone who wants to be our chief diplomat at the United Nations certainly give me pause ENDEND 
Mr. Chairman, there is no question that we need someone in New York who is unafraid to shake things up and challenge the status quo ENDEND  But, we also need someone with the credibility, temperament, and diplomatic skill to work with other nations, form coalitions, and advance United States   interests ENDEND 
That is why I've invoked the memory of Adlai Stevenson here today: he was tough; he was credible; he was diplomatic ENDEND  Most importantly, he was effective ENDEND  Stevenson proved that we should not make compromises or tradeoffs when selecting our Representative to the United Nations 
I want to give Mr. Bolton a chance to speak on these issues, and so my mind is not made up yet ENDEND  I want to hear from him as to how he can be an effective and credible advocate for the United States   I look forward to hearing his testimony and answers to the Committee's questions ENDEND

Almost eighty years ago, when the University of Chicago's first graduating class sat here ready to collect their diplomas, who would have dared to believe that before the beginning of the next century, we would add thirty years to the average lifespan and witness a  drop in the rate of infant death? Who would have dared to believe that with a simple vaccine, we could eliminate a disease that left millions without the ability to walk? That we could transplant a heart or resuscitate one that stopped? That we could unlock the greatest mysteries of life from the most basic building blocks of our existence?
In a time where you were lucky to live past fifty and doomed if you came down with the flu, who would have dared to believe these things?
The people who once sat in your chairs  they did ENDEND  The doctors and nurses, researchers and scientists who came before ENDEND  Who grew up believing that in America, the most improbable of all experiments, the place where we continue to defy the odds and write our own history, they could be the ones to improve, extend, and save human life ENDEND  They could be the healers ENDEND

Yet, while these are some of tomorrow's biggest potential breakthroughs, they are not today's biggest medical challenge ENDEND  Today, as we continue to find new ways to live longer and better, the greatest single threat to the health of our nation is not a scarcity of genius or a failure of discovery; it is a lack of collective will to ensure that every single American has access to effective, affordable health care ENDEND  It is our inability, after years and years of talk and gridlock, to finally do something about the crushing cost of health care in America ENDEND 
This has long stopped being about a single issue that politicians bring up during an election year ENDEND  This is now a national crisis ENDEND 
 million Americans are uninsured  over  million more in the last four years ENDEND  This isn't just a moral shame, it's an economic disaster that's catching Americans in a vicious cycle ENDEND  Because the uninsured can't afford health care, they put off seeing a doctor or end up in the ER when they get sick ENDEND  Then their care is more expensive, and so premiums for all Americans go up  to the tune of  a family ENDEND  Because everyone's premiums go up, more Americans lose their health care ENDEND 
All the while, costs just keep climbing and climbing ENDEND  Family premiums are up by nearly  over the last five years ENDEND  Deductibles are up  ENDEND  Copayments for care and prescriptions are through the roof ENDEND  From the smallest mom and pop stores to major corporations like GM, businesses who can't afford these rising costs cut back on insurance, workers, or both ENDEND  States with bigger Medicaid bills and smaller budgets are being forced to choose whether they want their citizens to be unhealthy or uneducated ENDEND  And over half of all family bankruptcies today are caused by medical bills ENDEND 
The cost crisis is affecting your profession too ENDEND  Whether it's Medicaid reimbursements, the rising price of medical malpractice insurance, or having HMOs look over your shoulder, all the hard work and sacrifice you've put in during medical school isn't as rewarding as it once was ENDEND 
So now, just like generations before, you must dare to believe  not only as tomorrow's physicians, but as tomorrow's parents, workers, business owners, and citizens ENDEND  You must choose: Will the groundbreaking miracles you discover over the next generation reach only the luckiest few? Or will history look back at this moment as the time when we finally made care available at a cost that won't bring the world's largest economy to its knees?
For you guys, this is about more than just the statistics and the numbers ENDEND  Part of the philosophy of the Pritzker School of Medicine has always been the recognition that "medicine does not exist in a social vacuum ENDEND " Living here over the last four years, you've seen this ENDEND  Surrounding us right now on the south side of Chicago are poor kids sitting in those ERs who could never afford a physical ENDEND  Children suffering from adult diabetes because parents can't afford to provide the proper nutrition ENDEND  Worried mothers thumbing through checkbooks, not knowing if they can pay for this month's medicine ENDEND 
There isn't one person sitting here today who wants to turn a sick patient away because they can't pay ENDEND  Not one person who wants the cure they discover denied to those whose lives depend on it ENDEND  Each of you has dedicated yourselves to this calling because where there is a sick person, you want to heal them ENDEND  Where there is a life in jeopardy, you want to save it ENDEND 
And so today, when you leave here, it will not only be with great knowledge, but with even greater responsibility ENDEND  Because if we do nothing about the rising cost of health care, experts believe that in ten years, the number of uninsured will grow to  million ENDEND  And if we do nothing to expand access to the uninsured, costs will keep rising ENDEND 
But while this is a national crisis, it still isn't part of the national conversation ENDEND  It's an issue where we haven't seen any leadership over the last four years; an issue that Washington continues to duck ENDEND  We just spent three entire weeks arguing over the filibuster, but I can count on one hand the number of times we've talked about health care since I was sworn in last January ENDEND  Yet, when I come back here and talk to families in Illinois, that's all they tell me about ENDEND 
This isn't just limited to one party either ENDEND  In part, the fear of reform stems from the experience President Clinton had in the early 's when he recognized the emerging health care crisis and sought a comprehensive answer ENDEND  For that, he deserves great credit ENDEND 
But the resulting political firestorm, fanned by the insurance lobby and other powerful interests who had a stake in maintaining the status quo, badly singed the Clinton Administration ENDEND 
Since then, health care continues to be fodder for candidates, but quickly recedes into the background when elections come and go ENDEND 
And with each passing year, the problem grows and the solutions become more difficult ENDEND 
We cannot turn our heads any longer ENDEND 
Challenging as it is, fixing the health care system is not an impossible problem to solve ENDEND  I'm not saying it will be easy, or that all the solutions are right in front of us ENDEND  We may not be able to build consensus on every detail right away, but where we do agree, we should act now to bring down skyrocketing costs ENDEND 
One place to start is by bringing the health care system into the st century ENDEND  In our lifetimes, we've seen some of the greatest advances in the history of technology and the sharing of information ENDEND  Yet, you're about to enter a profession where too much care is still provided with a pen and paper ENDEND  Where too much information about patients isn't shared between doctors or readily available to them in the first place ENDEND  And where we still don't have the information to know what care has worked most effectively and efficiently to make patients healthy ENDEND 
For too many Americans, the time lost as a result of archaic recordkeeping has been the margin between life and death ENDEND 
But embracing st century technology is not just about reducing errors and improving the quality of medical care ENDEND  It's also about cost ENDEND 
We spend nearly one and a half trillion dollars a year on health care in America ENDEND  But a quarter of that money  one out of every four dollars  is spent on nonmedical costs; most of it bills and paperwork ENDEND  Nearly every other industry in the world has saved billions on these administrative costs by doing it all online ENDEND  Every transaction you make at a bank now costs them less than a penny ENDEND  Even at the Veterans Administration, where it used to cost nine dollars to pull up your medical record, new technology means you can call up the same record on the internet for free ENDEND 
Yet, because we haven't updated technology in the rest of the health care industry, a single transaction still costs up to twentyfive dollars  not one dime of which goes toward improving the quality of our health care ENDEND  Doctors are forced to fumble through paperwork and don't have all of the information about each patient at the click of a mouse ENDEND  Mistakes are easily made and today, patients are given the right kind of care only half of the time ENDEND  In fact, a recent study showed that medical error alone kills up to , people a year  that's more than die from AIDS each year ENDEND  This is due not only to outdated medical technology, but the fact that we don't have a health care system that measures the quality of care patients are provided  that tells us what works, what doesn't, and where mistakes were made ENDEND 
But by bringing our health care system online, we could start improving the quality of care and cutting the cost of it ENDEND  We could save thousands of lives and save families billions of dollars ENDEND  Just imagine if every doctor and nurse could sit by a patient's bedside with a laptop and pull up their entire medical history  information from every past doctor they've seen  with the click of a mouse ENDEND  If every patient had an electronic bracelet that you could scan to find out the exact type and amount of medication they needed so there were no mistakes made ENDEND  If you could go online and monitor a patient's breathing and heart rate while they were home to track their recovery ENDEND 
We know all of this possible  so what are we waiting for? It's time for this country to start taking on the big challenges and asking the big questions again ENDEND  Why couldn't we help every health care provider who does business with the federal government that they need to convert their entire system to electronic transactions? Why couldn't we help connect our hospitals so they could share technology and information about what works and what doesn't? This way we could start creating a virtual system of health care in America where knowledge about each individual's health needs and health history is readily available to every single person who provides them care ENDEND 
And while we're at it, why couldn't we start rewarding the quality of care and the effort to prevent disease instead of only rewarding treatment itself? This way there would be actual incentives to provide good care and it would cut down on wasteful spending on the wrong kind of care ENDEND  As tomorrow's doctors, you can help here too by making sure that all the drugs you prescribe aren't just the latest pharmaceutical fad, but the cheapest and most effective medicine for your patients ENDEND 
So why couldn't we do all of this? The answer is, we can ENDEND  We can bring down the cost of health care in America and insure every American, and your generation can lead the way ENDEND 
Of course, no one's forcing you to meet these challenges ENDEND  Each of you has been blessed with extraordinary gifts and talent ENDEND  And so if you want, you can leave here and focus on your own medical career and your own success, not giving another thought to the plight of the growing millions who can't afford the care you will provide ENDEND  After all, there is no community service requirement in the real world; and no one's forcing you to care ENDEND

The other day I had the opportunity to visit some of our wounded heroes at Walter Reed Army Medical Center ENDEND

And now, some of them have come home from that war with scars that may change their lives forever  scars that may never heal ENDEND  And yet they sit there in that hospital, so full of hope and still so proud of their country ENDEND 
These kids are the best of America ENDEND  They deserve our highest respect, and they deserve our help ENDEND 
Recently, I learned that some of our most severely wounded soldiers are being forced to pay for their own meals and their own phone calls while being treated in medical hospitals ENDEND 
Up until last year, there was a law on the books that prohibited soldiers from receiving both their basic subsistence allowance and free meals from the military ENDEND  Basically, this law allowed the government to charge our wounded heroes for food while they were recovering from their war injuries ENDEND 
Thankfully, this body acted to change this law in  so that wounded soldiers wouldn't have to pay for their meals ENDEND 
But, we're dealing with a bureaucracy here, and as we all know, nothing is ever simple in a bureaucracy ENDEND  So now, because the Department of Defense doesn't consider getting physical therapy or rehabilitation services in a medical hospital as "being hospitalized," there are wounded veterans who still do not qualify for the free meals other veterans receive ENDEND  And after  days, even those classified as hospitalized on an outpatient status lose their free meals as well ENDEND 
Also, while our soldiers in the field qualify for free phone service, injured service men and women who may be hospitalized hundreds or thousands of miles from home do not receive this benefit ENDEND 
For soldiers whose family members aren't able to take off work and travel to a military hospital, hearing the familiar voice of a mom or dad or husband or wife on the other side of the phone can make all the difference in the world ENDEND 
And yet, our government will not help pay for these calls ENDEND  And it will not help pay for those meals ENDEND 
Think about that ENDEND  Think about the sacrifice these kids have made for their country, many of them literally risking life and sacrificing limb ENDEND 
And now, at  ENDEND  a meal, they could end up with a  bill from the government that sent them to war every single month ENDEND  This is wrong, and we have a moral obligation to fix it ENDEND 
The first amendment that I'm offering today will do this ENDEND  It will expand the group of "hospitalized" soldiers who cannot be charged for their meals to include those service members undergoing medical recuperation, therapy or otherwise on "medical hold ENDEND " The number of people affected by this amendment will be small ENDEND  Only about  service members are estimated to fall under the category of "nonhospitalized ENDEND "
The amendment is retroactive to January , , in an effort to provide those injured service members who may have received bills for their meals with some relief from those costs ENDEND  The amendment will also extend free phone service to those injured service members who are hospitalized or otherwise undergoing medical recuperation or therapy ENDEND  I am proud that this amendement is supported by the American Legion, and I hope my colleagues will join them in that support ENDEND 
I ask my colleagues to join me in supporting this amendment ENDEND  These are our kids out there, and they're risking their lives for us ENDEND  When they come home with injuries, the government that asked these kids to serve should provide them with the best possible care and support ENDEND  This is a small price to pay for those who have sacrificed so much for their country ENDEND

Let me congratulate all of those who have helped to make this wonderful vision a reality ENDEND 
But we gather here today not to celebrate a building ENDEND  We gather to celebrate a man ENDEND 
What is it that makes Lincoln such a seminal figure in our story? How is it that this man, born in the backwoods of Kentucky, with little formal education, homely and awkward, a man given to depression and wracked with selfdoubt, might come to represent so much of who we are as a people, and so much of what we aspire to be?
Some of it has to do with the trajectory of his life ENDEND  In his rise from poverty, his selfstudy and ultimate mastery of language and of law, in his capacity to overcome personal loss and remain determined in the face of repeated defeat  in all of this we see a fundamental element of the American character, a belief that we can constantly remake ourselves to fit our larger dreams ENDEND 
Some of it has to do with the sheer energy of the man, the railsplitter, axinhand, looking out at a frontier of hope and possibility ENDEND  Lincoln believed deeply in the American spirit of innovation and exploration that accepts no limits to the heights to which our nation might reach ENDEND 
In all of this  the repeated acts of selfcreation, the insistence that with sweated brow and calloused hands and focused will we can recast the wilderness of the American landscape and the American heart into something better, something finer  in all of this Lincoln embodies our deepest myths ENDEND  It is a mythology that drives us still ENDEND 
And yet what separates Lincoln from the other great men has to do with something else ENDEND  It's an issue of character that speaks to us, of moral resolve ENDEND  Lincoln was not a perfect man, nor a perfect president ENDEND  By modern standards, his condemnation of slavery might be considered tentative; his Emancipation Proclamation more a military document than a clarion call for justice ENDEND  He wasn't immune to political considerations; his temperament could be indecisive and morose ENDEND 
And yet despite these imperfections, despite his fallibility ENDEND  ENDEND  ENDEND indeed, perhaps because of a painful selfawareness of his own failings, etched in every crease of his face and reflected in those haunted eyes ENDEND  ENDEND  ENDEND because of this essential humanity of his, when it came time to confront the greatest moral challenge this nation has ever faced, Lincoln did not flinch ENDEND  He did not equivocate or duck or pass the challenge on to future generations ENDEND  He did not demonize the fathers and sons who did battle on the other side, nor seek to diminish the terrible costs of his war ENDEND  In the midst of slavery's dark storm and the complexities of governing a house divided, he kept his moral compass pointed firm and true ENDEND 
It serves us then to reflect on whether that element of Lincoln's character, and the American character  that aspect which makes tough choices, and speaks the truth when least convenient, and acts while still admitting doubt  remains with us today ENDEND  Lincoln once said that "character is like a tree and reputation like its shadow ENDEND  The shadow is what we think of it; the tree is the real thing ENDEND "
At a time when image all too often trumps substance, when our politics all too often feeds rather than bridges division, when the prospects of a poor youth rising out of poverty seem of no consequence to the powerful, and when we evoke our common God to condemn those who do not think as we do, rather than to seek God's mercy for our own lack of understanding  at such a time it is helpful to remember this man who was the real thing ENDEND  Lincoln reminds us that our essential greatness is not the shadow of sophistication or popularity, or wealth or power or fleeting celebrity ENDEND  It is the tree that stands in the face of our doubts and fears and bigotries, and insists we can do better ENDEND 
Today we come to celebrate not a building but a man ENDEND  And as that man called once upon the better angels of our nature, so is he calling still, across the ages, to summon some measure of that character, his character, in each of us, today ENDEND
