Four decades later, we find ourselves immersed in a mass media culture that is at once more vast and more wasteful than ever before ENDEND  And so once again, we find ourselves asking those in charge to serve the needs of a nation that has a higher calling than simply peddling indecency and materialism for profit ENDEND  We don't have to accept what we see today as inevitable ENDEND  We can all work together to make media a place where big ideas and great debates are communicated ENDEND  We owe this much to ourselves, and we certainly owe it to our children ENDEND  Thank you ENDEND 

Good morning ENDEND  I want to start by thanking American University and the Committee for Economic Development for hosting this panel today ENDEND  It's an honor to be here and an honor to be among such great company ENDEND 
Over one hundred years ago, at the dawn of the last century, the Industrial Revolution was beginning to take hold of America, creating unimaginable wealth in sprawling metropolises all across the country ENDEND 
As factories multiplied and profits grew, the winnings of the new economy became more and more concentrated in the hands of a few robber barons, railroad tycoons and oil magnates ENDEND  In the cities, power was maintained by a corrupt system of political machines and ward bosses ENDEND  And in the state of New York, there existed a young governor who was determined to give government back to the people ENDEND 
In just his first year, he had already begun to antagonize the state's political machine by attacking its system of favors and corporate giveaways ENDEND  He also signed a workers' compensation bill, and even fired the superintendent of insurance for taking money from the very industry he was supposed to be regulating ENDEND 
None of this sat too well with New York's powerful party boss, who finally plotted to get rid of the reformminded governor by making sure he was nominated for the Vice Presidency that year ENDEND 
What no one could have expected is that soon after the election, when President William McKinley was assassinated, the greatest fears of the corrupt machine bosses and powerbrokers came true when that former governor became President of the United States and went on to bust trusts, break up monopolies, and return the government to its people ENDEND 
His name, of course, was Theodore Roosevelt ENDEND  He was a Republican ENDEND  And throughout his public life, he demonstrated a willingness to put party and politics aside in order to battle corruption and give people an open, honest government that would fight for their interests and uphold their values ENDEND 
Today, we face a similar crisis of corruption ENDEND  And I believe that we deserve similar leadership from those in power as well ENDEND 
The American people are tired of a Washington that's only open to those with the most cash and the right connections ENDEND  They're tired of a political process where the vote you cast isn't as important as the favors you can do ENDEND  And they're tired of trusting us with their tax dollars when they see them spent on frivolous pet projects and corporate giveaways ENDEND 
It's not that the games that are played in this town are new or surprising to the public ENDEND  People are not naive to the existence of corruption and they know it has worn the face of both Republicans and Democrats over the years ENDEND 
Moreover, the underlying issue of how extensively money influences politics is the original sin of everyone who's ever run for office  myself included ENDEND  In order to get elected, we need to raise vast sums of money by meeting and dealing with people who are disproportionately wealthy ENDEND  This is a problem that predates George Bush or Jack Abramoff, and I believe that a serious, bipartisan conversation about campaign finance reform is one that this town would do well to have in the months to come ENDEND 
Yet, while people are familiar with these problems and they encompass both parties, I do think it's fair to say that the scandals we've seen under the current White House and Congress  both legal and illegal  are far worse than most of us could have imagined ENDEND 
Think about it ENDEND  In the past several months, we've seen politicians resigning for taking millions of dollars in bribes ENDEND  We've seen the head of the White House procurement office arrested ENDEND  We've seen some of our most powerful leaders of both the House and the Senate under federal investigation ENDEND  We've seen the number of registered lobbyists in Washington double since George Bush came into office ENDEND  And of course, we've seen the indictment of Jack Abramoff and his cronies ENDEND 
Now, there's an argument made that somehow this is a bipartisan scandal ENDEND  And the defense here is that everybody does it ENDEND  Well, not everybody does it ENDEND  And people shouldn't lump together those of us who have to raise funds to run campaigns but do so in a legal and ethical way with those who invite lobbyists in to write bad legislation ENDEND  Those aren't equivalent, and we're not being partisan by pointing that out ENDEND 
The fact is, since this Republican leadership has come to power, this kind of scandal has been the regular order of business in this town ENDEND  For years now, they have openly bragged about stocking K Street lobbying firms with former leadership staffers to increase their power in Washington ENDEND 
And yet, what is truly offensive to the American people about all of this goes far beyond people like Jack Abramoff ENDEND  It's bigger than how much time he'll spend in jail or how many Republicans he'll turn in ENDEND  Bigger than the K Street project and golf junkets to Scotland and lavish gifts for lawmakers ENDEND 
What's truly offensive about these scandals is that they don't just lead to morally offensive conduct on the part of politicians; they lead to morally offensive legislation that hurts hardworking Americans ENDEND 
Because when big oil companies are invited into the White House for secret energy meetings, it's no wonder they end up with billions in tax breaks while Americans still struggle to fill up their gas tanks and heat their homes ENDEND 
When a Committee Chairman negotiates a Medicare bill at the same time he's negotiating for a job as the drug industry's lobbyist, it's hardly a surprise when that industry gets taxpayerfunded giveaways in the same bill that forbids seniors from bargaining for better drug prices ENDEND 
When the people running Washington are accountable only to the special interests that fund their campaigns, of course they'll spend your tax dollars with reckless abandon; of course they'll load up bills with pet projects and drive us into deficit with the hope that no one will notice ENDEND 
In , over  ENDEND  billion was spent lobbying Congress ENDEND  That amounts to over  ENDEND  million per Member of Congress ENDEND   ENDEND  million per member so that oil companies can still run our energy policy and pharmaceutical companies can still raise our drug prices and special interests can still waste our tax dollars on pet projects ENDEND 
How much do you think the American people were able to spend on their Senator or Representative last year? How much money could the folks who can't fill up their gas tanks spend? How much could the seniors forced to choose between their medications and their groceries spend?
Not  ENDEND  million ENDEND  Not even close ENDEND 
This is the bigger story here, and this is why the recent scandals have shaken the American people's faith in a government that will look out for their interests and uphold their values ENDEND 
The wellconnected CEOs and hired guns on K Street who've helped write our laws have gotten what they paid for ENDEND  They got all the tax breaks and loopholes and access they could ever want ENDEND  But outside this city, the people who can't afford the highpriced lobbyists and don't want to break the law are wondering, "When is it our turn? When will someone in Washington stand up for me?"
We need to answer that call because let's face it  for the last few years, the people running Washington simply haven't ENDEND  And while only some are to blame for the corruption that has plagued this city, all are responsible for fixing it ENDEND 
Now, I've been asked by my caucus to take a role in lobbying reform  a role I'm proud to have ENDEND  As many of you know I'm from Chicago  a city that hasn't always had the cleanest reputation when it comes to politics in this country ENDEND  But during my first year in the Illinois State Senate, I helped lead the fight to pass Illinois' first ethics reform bill in twentyfive years ENDEND  I hope we can do something like that here ENDEND 
I realize there are many proposals floating around out there, and I also realize that our friends on the other side of the aisle have many of their own ENDEND  I think that's commendable ENDEND  In fact, I look forward to working in a bipartisan fashion to get a solid bill passed ENDEND 
But this has to be a serious bill, and it has to go a long way toward correcting some of the most egregious offenses of the last few years ENDEND  This is not a time for windowdressing or putting a bandaid on a problem just to score political points ENDEND  This is a time for real reform, and I think the Democrats' Honest Leadership and Open Government Act does this by including provisions that so far the Republican proposals do not ENDEND 
Real reform means making sure that Members of Congress and the Administration tell us when they're negotiating for jobs with industries they're responsible for regulating ENDEND  That way we don't have people writing a drug bill during the day and meeting with pharmaceutical companies about their future salary at night ENDEND 
Real reform means giving the public access to nowsecret conference committee meetings and posting all bills on the Internet  hours before they're voted on, so the public can scrutinize what's in them ENDEND 
Real reform means passing a bill that eliminates all gifts and meals from lobbyists, not just the expensive ones ENDEND  If the we truly agree that having a lobbyist constantly pick up the tab for lunch can help influence legislation, then they'll have no problem changing their position so that the ban includes meals of any price ENDEND 
Real reform means ending the nobid contracts for wellconnected contributors that have wasted millions of taxpayer dollars in both Iraq and the Gulf Coast ENDEND  And it means ending the practice of appointing your political buddies to positions they are wholly unqualified for ENDEND  It means no more Brownies ENDEND 
Finally, I think that real reform must include real oversight and accountability ENDEND  Our bill sets up an independent Office of Public Integrity to keep an eye on lobbyists and to make sure they comply with the rules ENDEND 
Now, personally, I think that there's an opportunity for us to go even further than some of the proposals that have come from both parties ENDEND  And that's why last week I introduced the CLEAN UP Act, which would build on the Democrats' reform bill by giving the American public a clearer view of what's going on here in Washington ENDEND 
See, one of the reasons why lobbyists like Abramoff and their allies in Congress have been able to manipulate the system is because most of their backroom deals are done in secret ENDEND  Just the other day, we heard that because of pressure from health care industry lobbyists, Republican negotiators met behind closed doors and changed a budget bill to provide a  billion giveaway to HMOs   billion that would come right out of the pockets of American taxpayers ENDEND  But of course, no one knew about the change until much later, and no lawmaker would admit to making it ENDEND 
This is an outrage, and my bill would change this by identifying secret provisions like these that weren't in the original bill, and it would let the public know who put them there, so that special interest giveaways couldn't be slipped in at the last minute ENDEND  My bill also would shine the spotlight on those pet projects that lawmakers sneak into every spending bill by requiring that they earmarks be posted on the internet  hours before they're voted on ENDEND  The watchdog group, Citizens for Responsibility and Ethics in Washington, recently endorsed this bill, and I hope that the Senate will take it up soon ENDEND 
Let me close with one final point ENDEND  Even if we pass a good bill and rid Washington of the Jack Abramoffs of the world, it's going to take much more than gift bans and lobbying reform to restore the public's faith in a government ENDEND  It will take not simply a change in laws, but a change in attitudes ENDEND 
To do this  to earn back that trust  to show people that we're working for them and looking out for their interests  we have to start acting like it ENDEND 
That means instead of meeting with lobbyists, it's time to start meeting with some of the  million Americans with no health care ENDEND 
Instead of finding cushy political jobs for unqualified buddies, it's time to start finding goodpaying jobs for hardworking Americans trying to raise a family ENDEND 
Instead of hitting up the big firms on K Street, it's time to start visiting the workers on Main Street who wonder how they'll send their kids to college or whether their pension will be around when they retire ENDEND 
All these people have done to earn access and gain influence is cast their ballot ENDEND  But in this democracy, it's all anyone should have to do ENDEND 
A century ago, that young, reformminded governor of New York who later became our twentysixth President gave us words about our country everyone in this town would do well to listen to today ENDEND  Teddy Roosevelt said that,
"No republic can permanently endure when its politics are corrupt and base ENDEND  ENDEND  ENDEND we can afford to differ on the currency, the tariff, and foreign policy, but we cannot afford to differ on the question of honesty ENDEND  There is a soul in the community, a soul in the nation, just exactly as their is a soul in the individual; and exactly as the individual hopelessly mars himself if he lets his conscience be dulled by the constant repetition of unworthy acts, so the nation will hopelessly blunt the popular conscience if it permits its public men continually to do acts which the nation in its heart of hearts knows are acts which cast discredit upon our whole public life ENDEND "
I can only hope that in the weeks to come, the work we do here and in Congress will once again strengthen this nation's soul and bring credit back to our public life ENDEND  Thank you ENDEND 

I want to thank the many people inside and outside of Congress who worked so hard over the past year to get us here ENDEND  We owe a debt of gratitude to the leadership on both sides of the aisle, and we owe special thanks to Chairmen Sensenbrenner and Specter, Ranking Members Conyers and Leahy, and Rep ENDEND  Mel Watt ENDEND  Without their work and dedication  and the support of voting rights advocates around the country  I doubt this bill would have come before us so soon ENDEND 
And I want to thank both chambers, and both sides of the aisle, for getting this done with the same broad support that drove the original Act  years ago ENDEND  At a time when Americans are frustrated with the partisan bickering that too often stalls our work, the refreshing display of bipartisanship we are seeing today reflects our collective belief in the success of the Act and reminds us of how effective we can be when we work together ENDEND 
Nobody can deny that we've come a long way since  ENDEND 
Look at registration numbers ENDEND  Only two years after passage of the original Act, registration numbers for minority voters in some states doubled ENDEND  Soon after, not a single state covered by the Voting Rights Act had registered less than half of its minority votingage population ENDEND 
Look at the influence of AfricanAmerican elected officials at all levels of government ENDEND  There are AfricanAmerican members of Congress ENDEND  Since , our nation's top diplomat has been an AfricanAmerican ENDEND 
In fact, most of America's elected AfricanAmerican officials come from the states covered by Section  of the Voting Rights Act  states like Mississippi and Alabama and Louisiana and Georgia ENDEND 
But to me, the most striking evidence of our progress can be found right across this building, in my dear friend, Congressman John Lewis, who was on the front lines of the civil rights movement, risking life and limb for freedom ENDEND  And on March , , he led  peaceful protestors demanding the right to vote across the Edmund Pettus Bridge in Selma, Alabama ENDEND 
I've often thought about the people on the Edmund Pettus Bridge that day ENDEND  Not only John Lewis and Hosea Williams leading the march, but the hundreds of everyday Americans who left their homes and their churches to join it ENDEND  Blacks and whites, teenagers and children, teachers and bankers and shopkeepers  a beloved community of God's children ready to stand for freedom ENDEND 
And I wonder, where did they find that kind of courage? When you're facing row after row of state troopers on horseback armed with billy clubs and tear gas ENDEND  ENDEND  ENDEND when they're coming toward you spewing hatred and violence, how do you simply stop, kneel down, and pray to the Lord for salvation?
But the most amazing thing of all is that after that day  after John Lewis was beaten within an inch of his life, after people's heads were gashed open and their eyes were burned and they watched their children's innocence literally beaten out of them ENDEND  ENDEND  ENDEND after all that, they went back to march again ENDEND 
They marched again ENDEND  They crossed the bridge ENDEND  They awakened a nation's conscience, and not five months later, the Voting Rights Act of  was signed into law ENDEND  And it was reauthorized in , , and  ENDEND 
Now, in , John Lewis, the physical scars from those marches still visible, is an original cosponsor of the fourth reauthorization of the Voting Rights Act, and he was joined last week by  of his House colleagues in voting for its passage ENDEND 
There are some who argue the Act is no longer needed, that the protections of Section 's "preclearance" requirement  a requirement that ensures certain states are upholding the right to vote  are targeting the wrong states ENDEND  But the evidence refutes that notion ENDEND  Of the , objections issued by the Department of Justice since ,  occurred since the last reauthorization in  ENDEND  So, despite the progress these states have made in upholding the right to vote, it's clear that problems still exist ENDEND 
Others have argued against renewing Section 's protection of language minorities ENDEND  Unfortunately, these arguments have been tied to the debate over immigration and muddle a noncontroversial issue  protecting the right to vote  with one of today's most contentious debates ENDEND 
But let's remember: you can't request language assistance if you're not a voter, and you can't be a voter if you're not a citizen ENDEND  And while voters, as citizens, must be proficient in English, many are simply more confident that they can cast ballots printed in their native languages without making errors ENDEND 
A representative of the Southwestern Voter Registration Project is quoted as saying: "Citizens who prefer Spanish registration cards do so because they feel more connected to the process; they also feel they trust the process more when they understand it ENDEND " These sentiments  connection to and trust in our democratic process  are exactly what we want from our voting rights legislation ENDEND 
Our challenges don't end at reauthorizing the Voting Rights Act either ENDEND  We have to prevent the problems we've seen in recent elections from happening again ENDEND  We've seen political operatives purge voters from registration rolls for no legitimate reason, prevent eligible exfelons from casting ballots, distribute polling equipment unevenly, and deceive voters about the time, location and rules of elections ENDEND  Unfortunately, these efforts have been directed primarily at minorities, the disabled, lowincome individuals, and other historically disenfranchised groups ENDEND 
The Help America Vote Act was a big step in the right direction, but we need to do more ENDEND  We need to fully fund HAVA ENDEND  We need to enforce critical requirements like statewide registration databases ENDEND  We need to make sure polling equipment is distributed equitably and that the equipment works ENDEND  And we need to work on getting more people to the polls on election day ENDEND 
We need to make sure that minority voters are not the subject of deplorable intimidation tactics when they do get to the polls ENDEND  In , Native American voters in South Dakota were confronted by men posing as law enforcement ENDEND  These hired intimidators joked about jail time for ballot missteps, and followed voters to their cars to record their license plate numbers ENDEND 
In Lake County, Ohio, some voters received a memo on bogus Board of Elections letterhead informing voters who registered through Democratic and NAACP drives that they could not vote ENDEND 
In Wisconsin, a flier purporting to be from the "Milwaukee Black Voters League" was circulated in predominantly AfricanAmerican neighborhoods with the following message: "If you've already voted in any election this year, you can't vote in the presidential election ENDEND  If you violate any of these laws, you can get ten years in prison and your children will get taken away from you ENDEND "
So, we have much more work to do ENDEND  This occasion is cause for celebration, but it's also an opportunity to renew our commitment to voting rights ENDEND  As Congressman Lewis said last week: "It's clear that we have come a great distance, but we still have a great distance to go ENDEND "
The memory of Selma still lives on in the spirit of the Voting Rights Act ENDEND  Since that day, the Voting Rights Act has been a critical tool in ensuring that all Americans not only have the right to vote, but the right to have their vote counted ENDEND  Those of us concerned about protecting those rights can't afford to sit on our laurels upon reauthorization of this bill ENDEND  We must take advantage of this rare united front and continue the fight to ensure unimpeded access to the polls for all Americans ENDEND  In other words, we need to take the spirit that existed on that bridge, and we have to spread it across this country ENDEND 
Two weeks after the first march was turned back, Dr ENDEND  King told a gathering of organizers and activists and community members that they should not despair because the arc of the moral universe is long, but it bends towards justice ENDEND  That's because of the work that each of us do to bend it towards justice ENDEND  It's because of people like John Lewis and Fannie Lou Hamer and Coretta Scott King and Rosa Parks, all the giants upon whose shoulders we stand that we are the beneficiaries of that arc bending towards justice ENDEND 
That's why I stand here today ENDEND  I would not be in the United States Senate had it not been for the efforts and courage of so many parents and grandparents and ordinary people who were willing to reach up and bend that arc in the direction of justice ENDEND  I hope we continue to see that spirit live on, not just during this debate, but throughout all our work here in the Senate ENDEND  Thank you ENDEND 

I want to start by saying how blessed I feel to be a part of today and how grateful I am for your church and your pastor, my friend Rick Warren ENDEND 
Ever since Rick and Kay visited Africa to see the pain and suffering wrought by AIDS, the Warrens and this church have proved each day that faith is not just something you have, it's something you do ENDEND  Their decision to devote their time, their money, and their purposedriven lives to the greatest health crisis in human history is not one that's always reported on the news or splashed across the front pages, but it is quietly becoming one of the most influential forces in the struggle against HIV and AIDS ENDEND  The resources of governments may be vast, and the good works of philanthropists may be abundant, but we should never underestimate how powerful the passion of people of faith can be in eradicating this disease ENDEND 
One of those passionate individuals is the man we just heard from  my friend and colleague, Sam Brownback ENDEND  Now, Sam and I may not agree on every issue, but I could not be more impressed with his efforts on issues like AIDS, the crisis in the Congo, the genocide in Darfur and sexual trafficking  issues that touch some of the world's most vulnerable people ENDEND  I am proud to work with him on many of these issues, and I'm proud to be by his side today ENDEND 
I took my own trip to Africa a few months ago ENDEND  As I'm sure Rick and Kay would agree, it's an experience that stays with you for quite some time ENDEND  I visited an HIVAIDS hospital in South Africa that was filled to capacity with people who walked hours  even days  just for the chance to seek help ENDEND  I met courageous patients who refused to give up for themselves or their families ENDEND  And I came across AIDS activists who meet resistance from their own government but keep on fighting anyway ENDEND 
But of all that I heard, I encountered few stories as heartbreaking as the one recently told by Laurie Goering, a Chicago Tribune reporter based in Johannesburg who had covered our trip for her newspaper ENDEND 
Three years ago, Laurie hired a woman named Hlengiwe Leocardia Mchunu as her nanny ENDEND  Leo, as she is known, grew up as one of nine children in a small South African village ENDEND  All through her life, she worked hard to raise her two kids and save every last penny she earned, and by the time Leo was hired as Laurie's nanny, she had almost finished paying off the mortgage on her home ENDEND  She had even hoped to use the extra money from her new job to open a refuge for local children who had been orphaned by AIDS ENDEND 
Then one day, Leo received a phone call that her eldest brother had fallen ill ENDEND  At first he told everyone it was diabetes, but later, in the hospital, admitted to the family it was AIDS ENDEND  He died a few days later ENDEND  His wife succumbed to the disease as well ENDEND  And Leo took in their three children ENDEND 
Six months later, Leo got another phone call ENDEND  Her younger brother had also become sick with AIDS ENDEND  She cared for him and nursed him as she did her first brother, but he soon died as well ENDEND 
Leo's pregnant sister was next ENDEND  And then another brother ENDEND  And then another brother ENDEND 
She paid for their caskets and their funerals ENDEND  She took in their children and paid for their schooling ENDEND  She ran out of money, and she borrowed what she could ENDEND  She ran out again, and she borrowed even more ENDEND 
And still, the phone calls continued ENDEND  All across her tiny village, Leo watched more siblings and cousins and nieces and nephews test positive for HIV ENDEND  She saw neighbors lose their families ENDEND  She saw a grandmother house sixteen orphaned grandchildren under her roof ENDEND  And she saw some children go hungry because there was no one to care for them at all ENDEND 
You know, AIDS is a story often told by numbers ENDEND   million infected with HIV ENDEND  Nearly  ENDEND  million this year alone ENDEND   million orphans in Africa ENDEND  , deaths and , new infections every single day ENDEND  In some places,  of those with HIV do not know they have it ENDEND  And we just learned that AIDS is set to become the rd leading cause of death worldwide in the coming years ENDEND 
They are staggering, these numbers, and they help us understand the magnitude of this pandemic ENDEND  But when repeated by themselves, statistics can also numb  they can hide the individual stories and tragedies and hopes of the Leos who live the daily drama of this disease ENDEND 
On this World AIDS day, these are the stories that the world needs to hear ENDEND  They are the stories that touch our souls  and that call us to action ENDEND 
I cannot begin to imagine what it would be like if Leo's family was my own ENDEND  If I had to answer those phone calls  if I had to attend those funerals ENDEND  All I know is that no matter how or why my family became sick, I would be called to care for them and comfort them and do what I could to help find a cure ENDEND  I know every one of you would do the same if it were your family ENDEND 
Here's the thing  my faith tells me that Leo's family is my family ENDEND 
We are all sick because of AIDS  and we are all tested by this crisis ENDEND  It is a test not only of our willingness to respond, but of our ability to look past the artificial divisions and debates that have often shaped that response ENDEND  When you go to places like Africa and you see this problem up close, you realize that it's not a question of either treatment or prevention  or even what kind of prevention  it is all of the above ENDEND  It is not an issue of either science or values  it is both ENDEND  Yes, there must be more money spent on this disease ENDEND  But there must also be a change in hearts and minds; in cultures and attitudes ENDEND  Neither philanthropist nor scientist; neither government nor church, can solve this problem on their own  AIDS must be an allhandsondeck effort ENDEND 
Let's talk about what these efforts involve ENDEND  First, if we hope to win this fight, we must stop new infections  we must do what we can to prevent people from contracting HIV in the first place ENDEND 
Now, too often, the issue of prevention has been framed in eitheror terms ENDEND  For some, the only way to prevent the disease is for men and women to change their sexual behavior  in particular, to abstain from sexual activity outside of marriage ENDEND  For others, such a prescription is unrealistic; they argue that we need to provide people with the tools they need to protect themselves from the virus, regardless of their sexual practices  in particular, by increasing the use of condoms, as well as by developing new methods, like microbicides, that women can initiate themselves to prevent transmission during sex ENDEND  And in the debate surrounding how we should tackle the scourge of AIDS, we often see each side questioning the other's motives, and thereby impeding progress ENDEND 
For me, this is a false argument ENDEND  Let me say this  I don't think we can deny that there is a moral and spiritual component to prevention  that in too many places all over the world where AIDS is prevalent  including our own country, by the way  the relationship between men and women, between sexuality and spirituality, has broken down, and needs to be repaired ENDEND 
It was striking to see this as I traveled through South Africa and Kenya ENDEND  Again and again, I heard stories of men and women contracting HIV because sex was no longer part of a sacred covenant, but a mechanical physical act; because men had visited prostitutes and brought the disease home to their wives, or young girls had been subjected to rape and abuse ENDEND 
These are issues of prevention we cannot walk away from ENDEND  When a husband thinks it's acceptable to hide his infidelity from his wife, it's not only a sin, it's a potential death sentence ENDEND  And when rape is still seen as a woman's fault and a woman's shame, but promiscuity is a man's prerogative, it is a problem of the heart that no government can solve ENDEND  It is, however, a place where local ministries and churches like Saddleback can, and have, made a real difference  by providing people with a moral framework to make better choices ENDEND 
Having said that, I also believe that we cannot ignore that abstinence and fidelity may too often be the ideal and not the reality  that we are dealing with flesh and blood men and women and not abstractions  and that if condoms and potentially microbicides can prevent millions of deaths, they should be made more widely available ENDEND  I know that there are those who, out of sincere religious conviction, oppose such measures ENDEND  And with these folks, I must respectfully but unequivocally disagree ENDEND  I do not accept the notion that those who make mistakes in their lives should be given an effective death sentence ENDEND  Nor am I willing to stand by and allow those who are entirely innocent  wives who, because of the culture they live in, often have no power to refuse sex with their husbands, or children who are born with the infection as a consequence of their parent's behavior suffer when condoms or other measures would have kept them from harm ENDEND 
Another area where we can make significant progress in prevention is by removing the stigma that goes along with getting tested for HIVAIDS ENDEND  The idea that in some places, nine in ten people with HIV have no idea they're infected is more than frightening  it's a ticking time bomb waiting to go off ENDEND 
So we need to show people that just as there is no shame in going to the doctor for a blood test or a CAT scan or a mammogram, there is no shame in going for an HIV test ENDEND  Because while there was once a time when a positive result gave little hope, today the earlier you know, the faster you can get help ENDEND  My wife Michelle and I were able to take the test on our trip to Africa, after the Center for Disease Control informed us that by getting a simple  minute test, we may have encouraged as many as halfamillion Kenyans to get tested as well ENDEND  Rick Warren has also taken the test ENDEND  Sam Brownback and I took it today ENDEND  And I encourage others in public life to do the same ENDEND  We've got to spread the word to as many people as possible ENDEND  It's time for us to set an example for others to follow ENDEND 
Of course, even as we work diligently to slow the rate of new infection, we also have a responsibility to treat the  million people who are already living with HIV ENDEND 
In some ways, this should be the easy part ENDEND  Because we know what works ENDEND  We know how to save people's lives ENDEND  We know the medicine is out there and we know that wealthy countries can afford to do more ENDEND 
That's why it was so frustrating for me to go to South Africa, and see the pain, and see the suffering, and then hear that the country's Minister of Health had promoted the use of beet root, sweet potato, and lemon juice as the best way to cure HIV ENDEND  Thankfully, the South African government eventually repudiated this, but it's impossible to overestimate how important it is for political leaders like this to set a good example for their people ENDEND 
We should never forget that God granted us the power to reason so that we would do His work here on Earth  so that we would use science to cure disease, and heal the sick, and save lives ENDEND  And one of the miracles to come out of the AIDS pandemic is that scientists have discovered medicine that can give people with HIV a new chance at life ENDEND 
We are called to give them that chance ENDEND  We have made progress  in South Africa, treatment provided to pregnant women has drastically reduced the incidents of infants born with the infection ENDEND  But despite such progress, only one in every five people with HIV around the world is receiving antiretroviral drug treatment ENDEND  One in every five ENDEND  We must do better ENDEND  We should work with drug companies to reduce the costs of generic antiretroviral drugs, and work with developing nations to help them build the health infrastructure that's necessary to get sick people treated  this means more money for hospitals and medical equipment, and more training for nurses and doctors ENDEND 
We need a renewed emphasis on nutrition ENDEND  Right now we're finding out that there are people who are on the drugs, who are getting treatment, who are still dying because they don't have any food to eat ENDEND  This is inexcusable  especially in countries that have sufficient food supplies ENDEND  So we must help get them that nutrition, and this is another place where religious organizations that have always provided food to the hungry can help a great deal ENDEND 
And even as we focus on the enormous crisis in Africa, we need to remember that the problem is not in Africa alone ENDEND  In the last few years, we have seen an alarming rise in infection rates in the Middle East, Southeast Asia, the former Soviet Union, Eastern Europe, and the Caribbean ENDEND  And on this World AIDS day, we cannot forget the crisis occurring in our own backyard ENDEND  Right here in the United States, AIDS is now the leading cause of death for African American women aged , and we are also seeing many poorer and rural communities fail to get the resources they need to deal with their vulnerable populations  a problem that unfortunately some in Congress are trying to address by taking money away from larger cities that are still facing enormous problems of their own ENDEND 
Now let me say this  I think that President Bush and this past Congress should be applauded for the resources they have contributed to the fight against HIV and AIDS ENDEND  Through our country's emergency plan for AIDS relief, the United States will have contributed more than  billion over five years to combat HIVAIDS overseas ENDEND  And the Global Fund, with money from the United States and other countries, has done some heroic work to fight this disease ENDEND  As I traveled throughout Africa this summer, I was proud of the tangible impact that all this money was having, often through coordinated efforts with the Centers for Disease Control, the State Department, foreign governments, and nongovernmental organizations ENDEND 
So our first priority in Congress should be to reauthorize this program when it expires in  ENDEND  Our second priority should be to reassess what's worked and what hasn't so that we're not wasting one dollar that could be saving someone's life ENDEND 
But our third priority should be to actually boost our contribution to this effort ENDEND  With all that is left to be done in this struggle  with all the other areas of the world that need our help  it's time for us to add at least an additional  billion a year in new money over the next five years to strengthen and expand the program to places like Southeast Asia, India, and Eastern Europe, where the pandemic will soon reach crisis proportions ENDEND 
Of course, given all the strains that have been placed on the United States   budget, and given the extraordinary needs that we face here at home, it may be hard to find the money ENDEND  But I believe we must try ENDEND  I believe it will prove to be a wise investment ENDEND  The list of reasons for us to care about AIDS is long ENDEND  In an interconnected, globalized world, the ability of pandemics to spread to other countries and continents has never been easier or faster than it is today ENDEND  There are also security implications, as countries whose populations and economies have been ravaged by AIDS become fertile breeding grounds for civil strife and even terror ENDEND 
But the reason for us to step up our efforts can't simply be instrumental ENDEND  There are more fundamental reasons to care ENDEND  Reasons related to our own humanity ENDEND  Reasons of the soul ENDEND 
Like no other illness, AIDS tests our ability to put ourselves in someone else's shoes  to empathize with the plight of our fellow man ENDEND  While most would agree that the AIDS orphan or the transfusion victim or the wronged wife contracted the disease through no fault of their own, it has too often been easy for some to point to the unfaithful husband or the promiscuous youth or the gay man and say "This is your fault ENDEND  You have sinned ENDEND "
I don't think that's a satisfactory response ENDEND  My faith reminds me that we all are sinners ENDEND 
My faith also tells me that  as Pastor Rick has said  it is not a sin to be sick ENDEND  My Bible tells me that when God sent his only Son to Earth, it was to heal the sick and comfort the weary; to feed the hungry and clothe the naked; to befriend the outcast and redeem those who strayed from righteousness ENDEND 
Living His example is the hardest kind of faith  but it is surely the most rewarding ENDEND  It is a way of life that can not only light our way as people of faith, but guide us to a new and better politics as Americans ENDEND 
For in the end, we must realize that the AIDS orphan in Africa presents us with the same challenge as the gang member in South Central, or the Katrina victim in New Orleans, or the uninsured mother in North Dakota ENDEND 
We can turn away from these Americans, and blame their problems on themselves, and embrace a politics that's punitive and petty, divisive and small ENDEND 
Or we can embrace another tradition of politics  a tradition that has stretched from the days of our founding to the glory of the civil rights movement, a tradition based on the simple idea that we have a stake in one another  and that what binds us together is greater than what drives us apart, and that if enough people believe in the truth of that proposition and act on it, then we might not solve every problem, but we can get something meaningful done for the people with whom we share this Earth ENDEND 
Let me close by returning to the story of Leo, that South African woman burdened by so much death and despair ENDEND  Sometime after the death of her fifth sibling, she decided that she wasn't just going to stand idly by ENDEND  She decided to call the town's first public meeting about the AIDS crisis  something that no one had even talked about, let alone met about ENDEND   people showed up ENDEND  Some had walked for miles to get there, a few with their grandchildren on their back ENDEND 
One by one, they stood up and broke their silence, and they told their stories ENDEND  Stories of tragedy, and stories of hope ENDEND  And when they were done, Leo rose and said, "I don't know whether we will win this war, but I'm looking for people who will stand up and face the reality ENDEND  The time for sitting silently has come to an end ENDEND "
Everything did not suddenly get better after that meeting, but some things did ENDEND  Despite all the children she had to raise and all the sick relatives she still had to care for, Leo still decided to open the AIDS orphanage she had dreamed about so long ago ENDEND  She began building a daycare center that would house one hundred orphans ENDEND  And she started plans on a youth center and a soup kitchen ENDEND 
I hear that part of the story and I think, if this woman who has so little, and has lost so much, can do so much good  if she can still make a way out of no way  then what are we waiting for?
Corinthians says that we are all of one spirit, and that "if one part suffers, every part suffers with it ENDEND " But it also says, "if one part is honored, every part rejoices with it ENDEND "
On this World AIDS day, it is the stories of overcoming, and not just illness, that the world needs to hear ENDEND  Yes, the stories of sadness call us to suffer with the sick ENDEND  But stories like Leo's also call us to honor her example, rejoice in the hope that it brings, and work to help her find that brighter future ENDEND  Thank you, and God Bless you ENDEND 

When Ryan was just two years old, his parents and doctors noted severe delays in his motor and speech development, and he was diagnosed with cerebral palsy ENDEND  His parents were devastated, as the prognosis for many children with cerebral palsy is quite grim, and given the severity of Ryan's condition, his doctors didn't have much hope for his improvement ENDEND 
Yet, his parents had hope ENDEND  Because when Ryan was born, his parents had saved his cord blood, a viable but limited source of stem cells ENDEND  They found a doctor at Duke University who was willing to perform an experimental infusion with these cells to see if they might improve his condition ENDEND 
They did ENDEND  In fact, they seem to have cured him ENDEND 
Within months of the infusion, Ryan was able to speak, use his arms, and eat normally, just like any other child  a miracle his family had once only dreamed of ENDEND 
Ryan's story exemplifies the power and the promise of stem cells to treat and cure the millions of Americans who are suffering from catastrophic, debilitating and lifethreatening diseases and health conditions ENDEND 
Each year, , Americans will develop Alzheimer's disease ENDEND  Over  million adults will be diagnosed with diabetes this year, which can lead to complications such as blindness, damaged nerves and loss of kidney function ENDEND  And there are far too many individuals with spinal cord injuries who are struggling to maintain mobility and independence ENDEND 
For most of our history, medicine has offered little hope of recovery to individuals affected by these and other devastating illnesses and injuries ENDEND 
Until now ENDEND 
Recent developments in stem cell research may hold the key to improved treatments, if not cures, for those affected by Alzheimer's disease, diabetes, spinal cord injury and countless other conditions ENDEND 
Many men, women and children who are cancer survivors are already familiar with the lifesaving applications of adult stem cell research ENDEND  Patients with leukemia or lymphoma often undergo bone marrow transplants, a type of stem cell transplant, which can significantly prolong life, or permanently get rid of the cancer ENDEND  This therapy has been used successfully for decades, and is saving lives everyday ENDEND 
Yet this breakthrough has its serious limitations ENDEND  Adult stem cells, such as those used in bone marrow transplants, can only be collected in small quantities, may not be a match for the patient, and have limited ability to transform into specialized cells ENDEND 
Cord blood, like the kind Ryan used, has limitations as well ENDEND  If, for example, young Ryan's condition should deteriorate or he should develop another illness, there simply are not enough cord blood cells left for a second use ENDEND  His mother has told us that the few remaining cells would have to be cloned to get enough cells for future use, or they would have to obtain stem cells from another source ENDEND 
These and other difficulties are the reasons why scientists have started to explore other types and other sources for stem cells, including embryonic stem cell research ENDEND 
Embryonic stem cells can be obtained from a number of sources, including in vitro fertilization ENDEND  At this very moment, there are over , embryos being stored in over  facilities throughout the United States ENDEND  The majority of these are reserved for infertile couples ENDEND  However, many of these embryos will go unused, destined for permanent storage in a freezer or disposal ENDEND  We should expand and accelerate research using these embryos, just as we should continue to explore the viability of adult stem cell use and cord blood use ENDEND 
All over the country, exciting progress is being made in the area of embryonic stem cell research ENDEND  At the University of Illinois, they're finding that stem cells have the potential to treat blood disorders, lung diseases, and heart damage ENDEND 
At Johns Hopkins, researchers were able to use mouse embryonic stem cells to repair damaged nerves and restore mobility in paralyzed rats ENDEND  One can't help but think that it's a matter of when, not if, this research will be able to one day help those who have lost the ability to walk ENDEND 
For these reasons, I'm proud to be a longterm supporter of greater stem cell research ENDEND  While I was a member of the Illinois Senate, I was the chief cosponsor of the Ronald Reagan Biomedical Research Act, which would specifically permit embryonic stem cell research in Illinois, and establish review of this research by the Illinois Department of Public Health ENDEND 
And I'm proud to be a cosponsor of the stem cell bill before us today ENDEND  This bill embodies the innovative thinking that we as a society demand and medical advancement requires ENDEND  By expanding scientific access to embryonic stem cells which would be otherwise discarded, this bill will help our nation's scientists and researchers develop treatments and cures to help people who suffer from illnesses and injuries for which there are currently none ENDEND  But the bill is not without limits; it requires that scientific research also be subject to rigorous oversight ENDEND 
I realize there are moral and ethical issues surrounding this debate ENDEND  But I also realize that we're not talking about harvesting cells that would've been used to create life and we're not talking about cloning humans ENDEND  We're talking about using stem cells that would have otherwise been discarded and lost forever  and we're talking about using those stem cells to possibly save the lives of millions of Americans ENDEND 
Democrats want this bill to pass ENDEND  Conservative, prolife Republicans want this bill to pass ENDEND  By large margins, the American people want this bill to pass ENDEND  It is only the White House standing in the way of progress  standing in the way of so many potential cures ENDEND 
I would only ask that the President thinks about this before he picks up his pen to deliver his first veto in six years ENDEND  I would ask that he thinks about Ryan Schneider and his parents, and all the other families who are sitting and waiting and praying for a cure  hoping that somewhere, a researcher or scientist will find the answer ENDEND 
There was a time in the middle of the last century when America watched helplessly as a mysterious disease left thousands  especially children  disabled for life ENDEND  The medical community worked tirelessly to try and find a cure, but they needed help  they needed funding to make their research possible ENDEND 
With a world war raging and the country still emerging from depression, the federal government could have ignored their plight or told them to find a cure on their own ENDEND 
But that didn't happen ENDEND  Instead, Franklin Delano Roosevelt helped galvanize a community of compassion and organize the March of Dimes to find the cure for polio ENDEND  And while Roosevelt knew that his own polio would never be cured by the discovery of a vaccine, he also knew that at its best, government can be used as a force to accomplish together what we cannot achieve on our own ENDEND 
And so the people began to care and the dimes piled up and the funding started to flow, and fifty years ago, Jonas Salk discovered the polio vaccine ENDEND 
Americans are looking for that kind of leadership today ENDEND  All over the country, patients and their families are waiting today for Congress and the President to open the door to the cures of tomorrow ENDEND  At the dawn of the st century, we should approach this research with the same passion and commitment that have led to so many cures and saved so many lives throughout our history ENDEND 
I urge my colleagues to support this bill ENDEND  Thank you ENDEND

As President Kennedy once said, “In serving his vision of the truth, the artist best serves his nation ENDEND ” Tonight, we honor five amazing artists who have dedicated their lives to telling their truth, and helping us to see our own ENDEND 
At eight years old, Mavis Staples climbed onto a chair in church, leaned into the microphone, raised her eyes upwards and belted out the gospel ENDEND  When people heard that deep, old soul coming out of that little girl, they wept  which, understandably, concerned her ENDEND  But her mother told her, “Mavis, they’re happy ENDEND  Your singing makes them cry happy tears ENDEND ”
It was those early appearances on the South Side of Chicago – South Side  with Mavis, her siblings, their father, Roebuck “Pops” Staples that launched the legendary Staple Singers ENDEND  Theirs was gospel with just a touch of country, a twist of the blues, little bit of funk ENDEND  There was a little bit of sin with the salvation ENDEND  And driven by Pops’ reverbed guitar, Mavis’ powerhouse vocals and the harmonies that only family can make, the Staple Singers broke new ground with songs like “Uncloudy Day ENDEND ” They had some truths to tell ENDEND  Inspired by Dr ENDEND  King, Pops would tell his kids, “If he can preach it, we can sing it ENDEND ” And so they wrote anthems like “Freedom Highway,” and “When Will We Be Paid”  which became the soundtrack of the Civil Rights movement ENDEND 
As a solo artist, Mavis has done it all and worked with just about everybody from Bob Dylan to Prince to Jeff Tweedy ENDEND  On albums like “We’ll Never Turn Back,” and “One True Vine,” she still is singing for justice and equality, and influencing a new generation of musicians and fans ENDEND  And each soulful note  even in heartbreak and even in despair – is grounded in faith, and in hope, and the belief that there are better days yet to come ENDEND  “These aren't just songs I'm singing to be moving my lips,” she says ENDEND  “I mean this ENDEND ” And we mean it too ENDEND  Six decades on, nobody makes us feel “the weight” like Mavis Staples ENDEND  Give her a big round of applause ENDEND 
Al Pacino calls the theater his “flashlight ENDEND ” It’s how he finds himself, where he sees truth ENDEND  And since Al first hit Broadway in , his singular talent has been the gold standard for acting ENDEND 
A great playwright once compared the way Al inhabits his characters to the way Louis Armstrong played jazz ENDEND  One director said that while “some actors play characters, Al Pacino becomes them ENDEND ” And we’ve all seen it ENDEND  In the span of five years  you think about it  he became Serpico, became Sonny Wortzik, twice became Michael Corleone for, let’s face it, what have got to be the two best movies of all time, became Tony Montana on screen, then became the owner of a couple of Tonys on stage ENDEND  And he’s always been this way ENDEND 
At , Al committed so profoundly to a role in the school play that when his character was supposed to get sick on stage, Al actually got sick on stage ENDEND  I’m not sure how audiences felt about that ENDEND  Later, when he played Richard III and Jackie Kennedy visited him backstage, the actor playing the selfabsorbed king didn’t even stand up to greet actual American royalty, which he says he still regrets ENDEND

In the late sixties, James Taylor got the chance to audition in front of Paul McCartney and George Harrison ENDEND  Ringo, I don’t know if you were there  but this is a true story ENDEND  “I was as nervous as a Chihuahua on methamphetamines" is what James Taylor says ENDEND  Which is exactly the kind of metaphor that makes him such a brilliant songwriter ENDEND

Without a preschool rivalry, we might not be honoring Martha Argerich ENDEND  The story goes that when Martha was two years old, a little boy taunted her, saying, “I bet you can’t play the piano” So she sat down at the keys, remembered a piece her teacher had played, and played it flawlessly ENDEND  By eight years old, she had made her concert debut ENDEND  By the time she was a teenager, she left her native Argentina to study in Vienna and won two major international competitions, launching one of the most storied and influential careers in classical music ENDEND  That little boy lost his bet ENDEND 
Martha combines unparalleled technical prowess with passion and glittering musicianship ENDEND  From Bach to Schumann, she doesn’t just play the piano, she possesses it ENDEND  Martha can charge through a passage with astonishing power and speed and accuracy, and, in the same performance, uncover the delicate beauty in each note ENDEND  As a critic once wrote, “She is an unaffected interpreter whose native language is music ENDEND ”
But what truly sets her apart and has cemented her place as one of the greatest pianists in modern history is her dogged commitment to her craft ENDEND  In an age of often superficial connections, where people too often seek fame and recognition, Martha has been guided by one passion, and that is fidelity to the music ENDEND  She can only be herself ENDEND  And that is the truest mark of an artist ENDEND  And the result is timeless, transcendent music for which we thank Martha Argerich ENDEND

Good evening, everybody ENDEND  Let me begin by thanking President Kuczynski and the wonderful people of Peru for hosting us and for their outstanding hospitality ENDEND  Peru is one of the United States’ strongest partners in the Americas  from standing up for democracy, to promoting jobs and growth, to fighting climate change ENDEND  And this summit has been a success thanks to the great work of our Peruvian friends ENDEND  So on behalf of us all, muchas gracias ENDEND 
This summit, and my trip over the past week, has obviously occurred against a backdrop of the broader debate over globalization and trade ENDEND  As I’ve said, over the decades, our global, integrated economy has helped to improve the lives of billions of people around the world with historic gains in prosperity, education, and health ENDEND  At the same time, when jobs and capital can move across borders, when workers have less leverage, when wealthy corporations and global elites too often seem to be playing by a different set of rules, then workers and communities can be hit especially hard; the gaps between the rich and everyone else grow wider ENDEND  And that can reverberate through our politics ENDEND 
That’s why I firmly believe that one of our greatest challenges in the years ahead  across our nations and within them  will be to make sure that the benefits of the global economy are shared by more people and that the negative impacts, such as economic inequalities, are addressed by all nations ENDEND  When it comes to trade, I believe that the answer is not to pull back or try to erect barriers to trade ENDEND  Given our integrated economies and global supply chains, that would hurt us all ENDEND  But rather the answer is to do trade right, making sure that it has strong labor standards, strong environmental standards, that it addresses ways in which workers and ordinary people can benefit rather than be harmed by global trade ENDEND  All of this is the central work of APEC ENDEND 
As this debate moves forward in the United States, it’s important to remember how vital the Asia Pacific is to America’s prosperity ENDEND  The  Asia Pacific economies here represent nearly three billion people, a majority of the global middle class, six of America’s top  trading partners, more than half of the global economy, and the world’s fastestgrowing region ENDEND  In other words, these  countries represent tremendous opportunity for the United States to sell our goods and support United States   jobs ENDEND  And that’s why, as part of the rebalance of our foreign policy to the Asia Pacific, we’ve boosted United States   exports to the region by some  percent ENDEND  Nearly  percent of our exports go to the region ENDEND 
And this is part of broader progress ENDEND  With  percent of the world’s customers beyond America’s borders, I’ve made it a priority to open up new markets overseas ENDEND  And during my Administration, we’ve increased United States   exports to the world by more than  percent  to record levels  and these exports support more than  million American jobs ENDEND  Moreover, we know that companies that export tend to grow faster and hire more employees and pay their workers more than companies that do not export ENDEND  All of which is why exports have helped to drive our economic recovery ENDEND  It's one of the reasons that United States   businesses have created more than  million new jobs ENDEND 
So that’s the kind of progress that trade  when done right  can deliver ENDEND  And that’s the kind of work that we’ve tried to do here at this summit ENDEND  We’re continuing our work to make it easier to do business between our countries so we’re creating even more jobs ENDEND  In the United States, we’re simplifying the process of starting a new business, increasing access to credit, all of which will help more ventures  especially small businesses  get up and running, and helping them to be able to export as well, so that they can access a global market even if they can't afford fancy lawyers and accountants and foreign offices ENDEND 
We agreed to increase our collective effort against corruption by targeting the bribery that enriches elites at the expense of people ENDEND  And we committed to making it easier to trade in services as well as goods ENDEND 
We also discussed the excess capacity that exists in certain sectors, like steel and aluminum that distorts markets and hurts business and workers, including American workers ENDEND  And even as I've argued that we cannot engage in protectionist measures, my Administration has been at the forefront of really cracking down hard on unfair trade practices and brought, consistently, cases against the  cases under the WTO against those who are engaging in unfair trading practices, and we've had a great track record of trade enforcement that has to be a part of this process ENDEND 
I’ve been very clear that excess capacity is not the result of market forces; it’s the result of specific government policies, and it needs to be fixed ENDEND  And here at APEC, we've been taking steps, as we were at the G in Hangzhou, to start addressing these issues in a systematic way ENDEND 
With regard to the digital economy, we endorsed rules to protect the privacy of personal information as it crosses borders ENDEND  We discussed the importance of maintaining the current moratorium on customs duties for digital goods and innovation ENDEND  And given growing cyber threats, our  APEC economies affirmed that no one should conduct or support cyberenabled theft of intellectual property, including trade secrets, with the intent to providing a competitive advantage to companies or commercial sectors ENDEND 
We’re also moving ahead with making our economies more inclusive ENDEND  And one particular area of focus is making sure that women have fair access to economic growth  expanding education; expanding access to careers in science, technology, engineering and math; helping more women entrepreneurs to access finance and integrate their businesses into the global supply chain ENDEND  According to one study, if women around the world participated in the labor force, it could add up to  trillion of additional output for the global economy   trillion ENDEND  When women are more prosperous, then families, communities and countries are all more prosperous as well ENDEND 
My meeting yesterday with my fellow leaders of the TransPacific Partnership was a chance to reaffirm our commitment to the TPP, with its high standards, strong protections for workers, the environment, intellectual property and human rights ENDEND  Our partners made very clear during the meeting that they want to move forward with TPP; preferably, they’d like to move forward with the United States ENDEND  A number of countries already are starting to ratify TPP ENDEND 
At the same time, we’re already hearing calls for a less ambitious trade agreement in the region with lower standards, lower protections for workers, lower protections for the environment ENDEND  That kind of agreement would obviously exclude United States   workers and businesses and access to those markets ENDEND  So for all those reasons, I believe that TPP is a plus for America’s economy, America’s workers, American jobs ENDEND  I think not moving forward would undermine our position across the region and our ability to shape the rules of global trade in a way that reflects our interests and our values ENDEND 
Finally, our cooperation with APEC has been critical to our historic progress in fighting climate change  bringing the Paris agreement into force, agreeing to limit aviation emissions, phrasing out dangerous HFCs ENDEND  Here in Lima, we continue our work to phase out fossil fuel subsidies, and countries made new commitments toward our goal of doubling our renewable energy over the next two decades ENDEND 
So, as I wrap up my last summit and likely my last foreign trip as President, I could not be more proud of the progress that we’ve made together ENDEND  Obviously, the work is never done ENDEND  And given the prosperity and security we seek for not only the United States but our allies and our partners, I continue to believe that America will have a vital role to play in creating and sustaining a strong, enduring leadership role in the Asia Pacific ENDEND

That was a rhetorical question, that first one ENDEND  Rather than comment on hypotheticals, let me say specifically what I did ENDEND  Obviously, my assets were significantly smaller than some other Presidents or President-elects ENDEND  But we made a decision to liquidate assets that might raise questions about how it would influence policy ENDEND

 Well, no, I'm not worried about being the last Democratic President ENDEND  I think  not even for a while ENDEND  And I say that, not being cute ENDEND  The Democratic nominee won the popular vote, and obviously this was an extremely competitive race and I would expect that future races will be competitive as well ENDEND  I certainly think it's true that politics in America right now are a little up for grabs, that some of the old alignments within both parties  Democrat and Republican  are being reshaped ENDEND  And although the results of this election involved some of the specifics of the candidates and aren't going to be duplicated in every subsequent election, Democrats do have to do some thinking about how do we make sure that the message we have is received effectively and results in winning elections ENDEND

Some of it is just political bad luck ENDEND  For example, I came in as an economy was in free fall, and although we took the right steps to save the economy, in my midterm election in , people couldn't yet see the recovery and, not surprisingly, the President’s party got punished ENDEND  We lost control of a lot of not just congressional seats but also gubernatorial seats and state legislative seats ENDEND  And that happened to be the year that the Census is done and you start doing redistricting, and those Republicans took advantage of political gerrymandering to lock in majorities, even though in numerous subsequent elections, Democrats have actually cast more votes  or more votes have been cast for Democratic congressional candidates than Republican ENDEND  And yet you end up having large Republican majorities ENDEND 
So there are just some structural problems that we have to deal with ENDEND  But, look, you can't make excuses about the rules ENDEND  That's the deal, and we got to do better ENDEND  And I think doing better, as I said, involves us working at the grassroots; not ceding territory; going out into areas where right now we may not stand a chance of actually winning but we're building up a cadre of young talent; we're making arguments; we're persuading; we're talking about the things that matter to ordinary people, day to day, and trying to avoid some of the constant distractions that fill up people’s Twitter accounts ENDEND 
And if we do that, then I'm confident that we'll be back on track ENDEND  I don't think that there has to be a complete overhaul here ENDEND  I think that there does have to be better organization, a smarter message ENDEND  And one message I do have for Democrats is that a strategy that's just microtargeting particular, discrete groups in a Democratic coalition sometimes will win you elections, but it's not going to win you the broad mandate that you need ENDEND  And ultimately, the more we can talk about what we have in common as a nation, and speak to a broad set of values, a vision that speaks to everybody and not just one group at a time, the better off we're going to be ENDEND

And in terms of reflecting on the United States   presidency as I've been traveling, I think the main reflection I have and the main advice that I give to the incoming President is the United States really is an indispensable nation in our world order ENDEND  And I say that as somebody who has gone out of his way to express respect for every country and its people, and to consistently acknowledge that many of the challenges that we face are not challenges that America can solve on its own ENDEND  But what I also know is that the basic framework of the world order coming out of World War II and then on through the end of the Cold War was shaped by a set of ideals and principles that have worked for the vast majority of people  not just America, but around the world ENDEND  The notion of democracy and rule of law, and a free press and independent judiciary, and open markets, and a social welfare state to moderate some off the sharp edges of capitalism, and lifting up issues of human rights, and investing in public health and development not just within our own borders but elsewhere in the world ENDEND  And working with multilateral institutions, like the United Nations; making sure that were upholding international norms and rules ENDEND 
That's what’s made the modern world ENDEND  And there have been times where we, ourselves, have not observed some of these norms as well as we should, and have been accused of hypocrisy ENDEND  Here in Latin America, there have been times where countries felt disrespected and, on occasion, had cause for that ENDEND  There are times where we haven't observed these values in our own country and have fallen short of our ideals ENDEND  But that basic structure is the reason why the world is much wealthier, much more secure, and, yes, less violent, healthier, better educated, more tolerant than it was  years ago ENDEND 
And that requires constant work ENDEND  It doesn’t just happen on its own ENDEND  I've said this in Europe ENDEND  I've said this in places where there’s this pushback against this modern order ENDEND  But you take an example like Europe  before that order was imposed, we had two world wars in the span of  years ENDEND  In the second one,  million people were killed ENDEND  Not half a million, not a million, but  million ENDEND  Entire continents in rubble ENDEND 
In places like the Asia Pacific, before that order existed, you routinely saw famines of millions of people  not just concerns about low wages, but people dying because they didn’t have any food or drinking water, or died of cholera or simple diseases  if somebody had some penicillin ENDEND 
And so what I would say would be that we all share responsibilities for improving that order and maintaining it, and making sure it's more inclusive, and delivers greater hope and prosperity for more corners of the world ENDEND  We all have responsibilities  every nation  in respecting the dignity and worth of their citizens ENDEND  And America can't do it all for everybody else ENDEND  There are limits to our reach into other countries if they’re determined to oppress their people, or not provide girls education, or siphon off development funds into Swiss bank accounts because they’re corrupt ENDEND  We're not going to be able to handle every problem ENDEND

 My guiding principle as President has been to try to do the right thing even when it’s not politically convenient; to look at long term trends in our economy, in our society, in the international sphere, and, using my best judgment, shape policies that will serve the American people, keep them safe, keep our economy growing, put people back to work, and best ensure peace, cooperation, and stability around the world ENDEND 
And based on current surveys of public opinion in the United States, it turns out that the majority of Americans think I’ve done a pretty good job, that we haven’t, in fact, gone too fast, as you describe it ENDEND 
But what is certainly true is that the American people  just like the German people, just like the British, and people around the world  are seeing extraordinarily rapid change ENDEND  The world is shrinking ENDEND  Economies have become much more integrated, and demographics are shifting ENDEND  Because of the Internet and communications, the clash of cultures is much more direct ENDEND  People feel, I think, less certain about their identity, less certain about economic security ENDEND  They’re looking for some means of control ENDEND 
And what that means is, is that the politics in all of our countries is going to require us to manage technology and global integration and all these demographic shifts in a way that makes people feel more control, that gives them more confidence in their future, but does not resort to simplistic answers or divisions of race or tribe, or the crude nationalism, which I think can be contrasted to the pride of patriotism that we all feel about our respective countries ENDEND 
And I think that our politics everywhere are going to be going through this bumpy phase ENDEND  But as long as we stay true to our democratic principles, as long as elections have integrity, as long as we respect freedom of speech, freedom of religion, as long as there are checks and balances in our governments so that the people have the ability to not just make judgments about how well government is serving them but also change governments if they're not serving them well  then I have confidence that over the long term, progress will continue ENDEND
