 I’m always optimistic ENDEND  There were times where I was in the Oval Office and people would come to me with all kinds of political problems and policy problems and international problems, and my team would be getting discouraged and depressed, and I would say to them, I have to be optimistic, because the odds of somebody named Barack Obama being President of the United States were very low, and the fact that, in my lifetime, I have seen such enormous, positive change in the United States and around the world tells me that, although history does not travel in a straight line, it moves in the direction of justice and freedom and a better life for people ENDEND  But we have to fight for it ENDEND  We have to work for it ENDEND 
What makes me cautiously optimistic about my successor and the shift from campaign mode to governance is there’s something about the solemn responsibilities of that office, the extraordinary demands that are placed on the United States  not just by its own people but by people around the world  that forces you to focus, that demands seriousness ENDEND  And if you’re not serious about the job, then you probably won’t be there very long because it will expose problems ENDEND 
Even when you’re doing a job, even when you are attentive, there are so many things that come across your desk that people are going to question you, and you’re going to have opponents and you’re going to have critics, and you figure that out pretty fast when you’re sitting there ENDEND  And I think the Presidentelect is going to see fairly quickly that the demands and responsibilities of a United States   President are not ones that you can treat casually, and that in a big, complex, diverse country, the only way that you can be successful is by listening and reaching out and working with a wide variety of people ENDEND 
And so it is my hope that that is what will happen ENDEND  And I’m going to do everything I can over the next two months to help assure that that happens ENDEND 
It is absolutely true that Chancellor Merkel is going to have significant responsibilities, has had extraordinary burdens that she’s had to carry ENDEND  If she chooses to continue, you're right, she will have big burdens ENDEND  I wish I could be there to lighten her load somewhat, but she’s tough ENDEND  And I have  I know what it means to carry burdens because the fact of the matter is, is that if there are problems around the world, the first question people ask is, why isn’t Washington doing something about it?
This is why it’s so important not to discount or take for granted the importance of the Transatlantic Alliance ENDEND  And this is probably the best place for me to end ENDEND 
In international for a  in Gs, in Gs, in the United Nations  the United States and Germany are not always perfectly aligned ENDEND  America and Europe are not always perfectly aligned ENDEND  But the voice that speaks out on behalf of some dissident who is jailed halfway around the world, the voice who is expressing concern about some child in an African village who doesn't have clean drinking water or is subject to some terrible disease, the voice that insists on rules and norms governing international affairs, the voice that helps to steer the world away from war wherever possible  that's our voice more often than not ENDEND 
And we're not always successful ENDEND  But if that voice is absent, or if that voice is divided, we will be living in a meaner, harsher, more troubled world ENDEND  And we have to remember that ENDEND  And whoever is the United States   President, and whoever is the Chancellor of Germany, and whoever is the leader of other European nations and other democracies around the world  they need to recognize that ENDEND 
There are going to be forces that argue for cynicism, for looking the other way with somebody else’s problems, that are not going to champion people who are vulnerable because sometimes that's politically convenient ENDEND  And if we don't have a strong transatlantic alliance that's standing up for those things, we will be giving to our children a worse world ENDEND  We will go backwards instead of forwards ENDEND 
So whoever the United States   President is, whoever the Chancellor of Germany is, we need to remember that ENDEND  And our citizenry who decide who our Presidents and Chancellors are need to remember that ENDEND 
Chancellor Merkel:  It is, after all, a very good thing if, after eight years of cooperation, the President of the United States says that this is a cooperation based on friendship, that we cooperated well ENDEND  I feel that this is a very good, a very positive message, and, indeed, an encouragement for me ENDEND 
Now, secondly, I, fortunately, know very many people  and there are many, many more that I don't know, and many politicians  who stand up for the same values of democracy, of liberal societies, of open societies, of respect for the dignity of man ENDEND  And I feel that we are in a community of people here who stand up for these values, who try to maintain them, and wherever they are not yet respected, stand up for people’s rights to enjoy them, as well ENDEND 
And this is worth every effort ENDEND  But I think we're gratified to know that there are many, many people who feel committed to this goal ENDEND 
Thank you very much ENDEND 

Hello, Greece Yia sas Kalispera  To the government and the people of Greece  including Prime Minister Tsipras, who I thank for his partnership and for being here, along with so many young people, the future of Greece  I want to thank you for your warm and generous welcome ENDEND 
As many of you know, this is my final trip overseas as President of the United States, and I was determined, on my last trip, to come to Greece  partly because I’ve heard about the legendary hospitality of the Greek people  your philoxenia ENDEND  Partly because I had to see the Acropolis and the Parthenon ENDEND  But also because I came here with gratitude for all that Greece  “this small, great world”  has given to humanity through the ages ENDEND 
Our hearts have been moved by the tragedies of Aeschylus and Euripides ENDEND  Our minds have been opened by the histories of Herodotus and Thucydides ENDEND  Our understanding of the world and our place in it has been expanded by Socrates and Aristotle ENDEND 
In the United States, we’re especially grateful for the friendship of so many proud Greek Americans ENDEND  In my hometown of Chicago  you can find them in Greektown, with their fustanellas ENDEND  And together, we’ve celebrated Greek Independence Day at the White House ENDEND  We’ve had some spanakopita and some ouzo ENDEND  Greek Americans have worn the uniform to keep our country free ENDEND  Greek Americans have marched with Dr ENDEND  Martin Luther King, Jr ENDEND  to make us more just ENDEND  Greek or American, we’re all cheering for Giannis Antetokounmpo  who seems to be getting better each year ENDEND  And if anyone seeks an example of our shared spirit, our resilience, they need look no further than New York City, near Ground Zero, where the Greek Orthodox Church of St ENDEND  Nicholas, once in ruins, is now rising again ENDEND 
Most of all, we’re indebted to Greece for the most precious of gifts  the truth, the understanding that as individuals of free will, we have the right and the capacity to govern ourselves ENDEND  For it was here,  centuries ago, in the rocky hills of this city, that a new idea emerged ENDEND  Demokratia ENDEND  Kratos  the power, the right to rule  comes from demos  the people ENDEND  The notion that we are citizens  not servants, but stewards of our society ENDEND  The concept of citizenship  that we have both rights and responsibilities ENDEND  The belief in equality before the law  not just for a few, but for the many; not just for the majority, but also the minority ENDEND  These are all concepts that grew out of this rocky soil ENDEND 
Of course, the earliest forms of democracy here in Athens were far from perfect  just as the early forms of democracy in the United States were far from perfect ENDEND  The rights of ancient Athens were not extended to women or to slaves ENDEND  But Pericles explained, “our constitution favors the many instead of the few…this is why it is called a democracy ENDEND ”
Athenians also knew that, however noble, ideas alone were not enough ENDEND  To have meaning, principles must be enshrined in laws and protected by institutions, and advanced through civic participation ENDEND  And so they gathered in a great assembly to debate and decide affairs of state, each citizen with the right to speak, casting their vote with a show of hands, or choosing a pebble  white for yes, black for no ENDEND  Laws were etched in stone for all to see and abide by ENDEND  Courts, with citizen jurors, upheld that rule of law ENDEND 
Politicians weren’t always happy because sometimes the stones could be used to ostracize, banish those who did not behave themselves ENDEND 
But across the millennia that followed, different views of power and governance have often prevailed ENDEND  Throughout human history, there have been those who argue that people cannot handle democracy, that they cannot handle selfdetermination, they need to be told what to do ENDEND  A ruler has to maintain order through violence or coercion or an iron fist ENDEND  There’s been a different concept of government that says might makes right, or that unchecked power can be passed through bloodlines ENDEND  There’s been the belief that some are superior by virtue of race or faith or ethnicity, and those beliefs so often have been used to justify conquest and exploitation and war ENDEND 
But through all this history, the flame first lit here in Athens never died ENDEND  It was ultimately nurtured by a great Enlightenment ENDEND  It was fanned by America’s founders, who declared that “We, the People” shall rule; "that all men are created equal" and "endowed by  Creator with certain inalienable rights ENDEND "
Now, at times, even today, those ideals are challenged ENDEND  We’ve been told that these are Western ideals ENDEND  We’ve been told that some cultures are not equipped for democratic governance and actually prefer authoritarian rule ENDEND  And I will say that after eight years of being President of the United States, having traveled around the globe, it is absolutely true that every country travels its own path; every country has its own traditions ENDEND  But what I also believe, after eight years, is that the basic longing to live with dignity, the fundamental desire to have control of our lives and our future, and to want to be a part of determining the course of our communities and our nations  these yearnings are universal ENDEND  They burn in every human heart ENDEND 
It’s why a Greek bishop atop a mountain raised the flag of independence ENDEND  It’s why peoples from the Americas to Africa to Asia threw off the yoke of colonialism ENDEND  It’s why people behind an Iron Curtain marched in Solidarity, and tore down that wall, and joined you in a great union of democracies ENDEND  It’s why, today, we support the right of Ukrainians to choose their own destiny; why we partner with Tunisians and the people of Myanmar as they make historic transitions to democracy ENDEND 
This has been my foreign policy during my presidency ENDEND  By necessity, we work with all countries, and many of them are not democracies ENDEND  Some of them are democracies in the sense they have elections, but not democracies in the sense of actually permitting participation and dissent ENDEND  But our trajectory as a country has been to support the efforts of those who believe in selfgovernance, who believe in those ideas that began here so many years ago ENDEND 
And it is not simply a matter of us being true to our values ENDEND  It’s not just a matter of idealism ENDEND  I believe it is practical for the United States to support democracies ENDEND  Because history shows us that countries with democratic governance tend to be more just, and more stable, and more successful ENDEND 
Open, democratic societies can deliver more prosperity because when people are free to think for themselves and share ideas and discover and create  the young people who are here, what they’re able to do through the Internet and technology, that’s when innovation is unleashed, when economies truly flourish ENDEND  That’s when new products, and new services, and new ideas wash through an economy ENDEND  In contrast to regimes that rule by coercion, democracies are rooted in consent of the governed  citizens know that there’s a path for peaceful change, including the moral force of nonviolence ENDEND  And that brings a stability that so often can facilitate economic growth ENDEND 
The history of the past two centuries indicates that democracies are less likely to fight wars among themselves ENDEND  So more democracy is good for the people of the world, but it’s also good for our national security ENDEND  Which is why America’s closest friends are democracies  like Greece ENDEND  It’s why we stand together in NATO  an alliance of democracies ENDEND 
In recent years, we’ve made historic investments in NATO, increased America’s presence in Europe, and today’s NATO  the world’s greatest alliance  is as strong and as ready as it’s ever been ENDEND  And I am confident that just as America’s commitment to the transatlantic alliance has endured for seven decades whether it’s been under a Democratic or Republican Administration  that commitment will continue, including our pledge and our treaty obligation to defend every ally ENDEND 
Our democracies show that we’re stronger than terrorists, and fundamentalists, and absolutists who can’t tolerate difference, can’t tolerate ideas that vary from their own, who try to change people’s way of life through violence and would make us betray or shrink from our values ENDEND  Democracy is stronger than organizations like ISIL ENDEND 
Because our democracies are inclusive, we’re able to welcome people and refugees in need to our countries ENDEND  And nowhere have we seen that compassion more evident than here in Greece ENDEND  The Greek people’s generosity towards refugees arriving on your shores has inspired the world ENDEND  That doesn’t mean that you should be left on your own  and only a truly collective response by Europe and the world can ensure that these desperate people receive the support that they need ENDEND  Greece cannot be expected to bear the bulk of the burden alone  but the fact that your democracy opens your heart to people in need in a way that might not otherwise be the case ENDEND 
Just as democracies are premised on the peaceful resolution of disagreements within our societies, we also believe that cooperation and dialogue is the best way to address challenges between nations ENDEND  And so it is my belief that democracies are more likely to try to resolve conflicts between nations in a way that does not result in war ENDEND  That’s how, with diplomacy, we were able to shut down Iran’s nuclear weapons program without firing a shot ENDEND  With diplomacy, the United States opened relations with Cuba ENDEND  With diplomacy, we joined Greece and nearly  nations in the most ambitious agreement ever to save our planet from climate change ENDEND 
And speaking of climate change, I would point out that there is a connection between democracy and science ENDEND  The premise of science is that we observe and we test our hypotheses, our ideas ENDEND  We base decisions on facts, not superstition; not what our ideology tells us, but rather what we can observe ENDEND  And at a time when the globe is shrinking and more and more we’re going to have to take collective action to deal with problems like climate change, the presence of a democratic debate allows the science to flourish and to shape our collective responses ENDEND 
Now, democracy, like all human institutions, is imperfect ENDEND  It can be slow; it can be frustrating; it can be hard; it can be messy ENDEND  Politicians tend to be unpopular in democracies, regardless of party, because, by definition, democracies require that you don’t get a hundred percent of what you want ENDEND  It requires compromise ENDEND  Winston Churchill famously said "that democracy is the worst form of government"  except for all the others ENDEND  And in a multiethnic, multiracial, multicultural society, like the United States, democracy can be especially complicated ENDEND  Believe me, I know ENDEND 
But it is better than the alternatives because it allows us to peacefully work through our differences and move closer to our ideals ENDEND  It allows us to test new ideas and it allows us to correct for mistakes ENDEND  Any action by a President, or any result of an election, or any legislation that has proven flawed can be corrected through the process of democracy ENDEND 
And throughout our history, it’s how we have come to see that all people are created equal  even though, when we were founded, that was not the case ENDEND  We could work to expand the rights that were established in our founding to African Americans, and to women, to Americans with disabilities, to Native Americans; why all Americans now have the freedom to marry the person they love ENDEND  It’s why we welcome people of all races and all religions and all backgrounds, and immigrants who strive to give their children a better life and who make our country stronger ENDEND 
And so here, where democracy was born, we affirm once more the rights and the ideals and the institutions upon which our way of life endures: freedom of speech and assembly  because true legitimacy can only come from the people, who must never be silenced; a free press to expose injustice and corruption and hold leaders accountable; freedom of religion  because we’re all equal in the eyes of God; independent judiciaries to uphold rule of law and human rights; separation of powers to limit the reach of any one branch of government; free and fair elections  because citizens must be able to choose their own leaders, even if your candidate doesn’t always win ENDEND 
We compete hard in campaigns in America and here in Greece ENDEND  But after the election, democracy depends on a peaceful transition of power, especially when you don’t get the result you want ENDEND 
And as you may have noticed, the next American President and I could not be more different ENDEND  We have very different points of view, but American democracy is bigger than any one person ENDEND  That’s why we have a tradition of the outgoing President welcoming the new one in  as I did last week ENDEND  And why, in the coming weeks, my Administration will do everything we can to support the smoothest transition possible  because that’s how democracy has to work ENDEND 
And that’s why, as hard as it can be sometimes, it’s important for young people, in particular, who are just now becoming involved in the lives of their countries, to understand that progress follows a winding path  sometimes forward, sometimes back  but as long as we retain our faith in democracy, as long as we retain our faith in the people, as long as we don’t waver from those central principles that ensure a lively, open debate, then our future will be okay, because it remains the most effective form of government ever devised by man ENDEND 
It is true, of course, over the last several years that we’ve seen democracies faced with serious challenges ENDEND  And I want to mention two that have an impact here in Greece, haven an impact in the United States, and are having an impact around the world ENDEND 
The first involves the paradox of a modern, global economy ENDEND  The same forces of globalization and technology and integration that have delivered so much progress, have created so much wealth, have also revealed deep fault lines ENDEND  Around the world, integration and closer cooperation, and greater trade and commerce, and the Internet  all have improved the lives of billions of people  lifted families from extreme poverty, cured diseases, helped people live longer, gave them more access to education and opportunity than at any time in human history ENDEND 
I’ve often said to young people in the United States, if you had to choose a moment in history to be born, and you did not know ahead of time who you would be  you didn’t know whether you were going to be born into a wealthy family or a poor family, what country you’d be born, whether you were going to be a man or a woman  if you had to choose blindly what moment you’d want to be born: You’d choose now ENDEND  Because the world has never, collectively, been wealthier, better educated, healthier, less violent than it is today ENDEND  That’s hard to imagine, given what we see in the news, but it’s true ENDEND  And a lot of that has to do with the developments of a integrated, global economy ENDEND 
But trends underway for decades have meant that in many countries and in many communities there have been enormous disruptions ENDEND  Technology and automation mean that goods can be produced with fewer workers ENDEND  It means jobs and manufacturing can move across borders where wages are lower or rights are less protected ENDEND  And that means that workers and unions oftentimes have less leverage to bargain for better wages, better benefits, have more difficulty competing in the global marketplace ENDEND  Hardworking families worry their kids may not be better off than they were because of this global competition ENDEND 
What we’ve also seen is that this global integration is increasing the tendencies towards inequality, both between nations and within nations, at an accelerated pace ENDEND  And when we see people  global elites, wealthy corporations  seemingly living by a different set of rules, avoiding taxes, manipulating loopholes  when the rich and the powerful appear to game the system and accumulate vast wealth while middle and workingclass families struggle to make ends meet, this feeds a profound sense of injustice and a feeling that our economies are increasingly unfair ENDEND 
This inequality now constitutes one of the greatest challenges to our economies and to our democracies ENDEND  An inequality that was once tolerated because people didn’t know how unequal things were now won’t be tolerated because everybody has a cellphone and can see how unequal things are ENDEND  The awareness that people have in the smallest African village, they can see how people in London or New York are living ENDEND  The poorest child in any of our countries now has a sense of what other people have that they don’t ENDEND  So not only is there increasing inequality, but also there is greater awareness of inequality ENDEND  And that’s a volatile mix for our democracies ENDEND 
And this is why addressing inequality has been one of the key areas of focus for my economic policy ENDEND  In our countries, in America and in most advanced market economies, we want people to be rewarded for their achievement ENDEND  We think that people should be rewarded if they come up with a new product or a new service that is popular and helps a lot of people ENDEND  But when a CEO of a company now makes more money in a single day than a typical worker does in an entire year, when it’s harder for workers to climb their way up the economic ladder, when they see a factory close that used to support an entire city or town, fuels the feeling that globalization only benefits those at the top ENDEND  And the reaction can drag down a country’s growth and make recessions more likely ENDEND  It can also lead to politics that create an unhealthy competition between countries ENDEND  Rather than a winwin situation, people perceive that if you’re winning, I’m losing, and barriers come up and walls come up ENDEND 
And in advanced economies, there are at times movements from both the left and the right to put a stop to integration, and to push back against technology, and to try to bring back jobs and industries that have been disappearing for decades ENDEND  So this impulse to pull back from a globalized world is understandable ENDEND  If people feel that they’re losing control of their future, they will push back ENDEND  We have seen it here in Greece ENDEND  We’ve seen it across Europe ENDEND  We’ve seen it in the United States ENDEND  We saw it in the vote in Britain to leave the EU ENDEND 
But given the nature of technology, it is my assertion that it’s not possible to cut ourselves off from one another ENDEND  We now are living in a global supply chain ENDEND  Our growth comes through innovation and ideas that are crossing borders all the time ENDEND  The jobs of tomorrow will inevitably be different from the jobs of the past ENDEND  So we can’t look backwards for answers, we have to look forward ENDEND 
We cannot sever the connections that have enabled so much progress and so much wealth ENDEND  For when competition for resources is perceived as zerosum, we put ourselves on a path to conflict both within countries and between countries ENDEND  So I firmly believe that the best hope for human progress remains open markets combined with democracy and human rights ENDEND  But I have argued that the current path of globalization demands a course correction ENDEND  In the years and decades ahead, our countries have to make sure that the benefits of an integrated global economy are more broadly shared by more people, and that the negative impacts are squarely addressed ENDEND 
And we actually know the path to building more inclusive economies ENDEND  It’s just we too often don’t have the political will or desire to get it done ENDEND  We know we need bold policies that spur growth and support jobs ENDEND  We know that we need to give workers more leverage and better wages, and that, in fact, if you give workers better wages businesses do better, too, because their customers now have money to spend ENDEND 
We know that we have to invest more in our people  the education of our young people, the skills and training to compete in the global economy ENDEND  We have to make sure that it is easy for young people who are eager to learn and eager to work to get the education that they need, the training that they need, without taking on huge amounts of debt ENDEND 
We know that we have to encourage entrepreneurship so that it’s easier to start a business and do business ENDEND  We know that we have to strengthen the social compact so that the safety net that is available for people, including quality health care and retirement benefits, are there even if people aren’t working in the same job for  years, or  years, or  years ENDEND 
We have to modernize our infrastructure, which will put people back to work ENDEND  We have to commit to the science and research and development that sparks new industries ENDEND 
In our trading relationships, we have to make sure that trade works for us, and not against us ENDEND  And that means insisting on high standards in all countries to support jobs, strong protections for workers, strong protections for the environment, so that even as we freely trade, people and workers in all countries see the benefits of trade in their own lives, not just benefits for the bottom line of large, multinational corporations ENDEND 
These are the kinds of policies, this is the work that I’ve pursued throughout my time as President ENDEND  Keep in mind I took office in the midst of the worst crisis since the Great Depression ENDEND  And we pursued a recovery that has been shared now by the vast majority of Americans ENDEND  We put people back to work building bridges and roads ENDEND  We passed tax cuts for the middle class ENDEND  We asked the wealthiest Americans to pay a little more taxes  their fair share ENDEND  We intervened to save our auto industry, but insisted that the auto industry become more energy efficient, produce better cars that reduce pollution ENDEND 
We put in place policies to help students with loans and protect consumers from fraud ENDEND  We passed the strongest Wall Street reforms in history so that the excesses and abuses that triggered the global financial crisis never happen again  or at least don’t start on Wall Street ENDEND 
And today, our businesses have created more than  million new jobs ENDEND  Incomes last year in America rose faster than any time since  ENDEND  Poverty fell at the fastest rate since  ENDEND  Inequality is being narrowed ENDEND  And we’ve also begun to close the pay gap between men and women ENDEND 
We declared that health care in America is a privilege not for the few, but a right for everybody ENDEND  Today our uninsured rate is at the lowest levels on record ENDEND  And we’ve done all this while doubling our production of clean energy, lowering our carbon pollution faster than any advanced nation ENDEND  So we’ve proven that you can grow the economy and reduce the carbon emissions that cause climate change at the same time ENDEND 
Now, I say all this not because we’ve solved every problem ENDEND  Our work is far from complete ENDEND  There are still too many people in America who are worried about their futures ENDEND  Still too many people who are working at wages that don’t get them above the poverty line ENDEND  Still too many young people who don’t see opportunity ENDEND  But the policies I describe point the direction for where we need to go in building inclusive economies ENDEND  And that’s how democracies can deliver the prosperity and hope that our people need ENDEND  And when people have opportunity and they feel confidence the future, they are less likely to turn on each other and they’re less likely to appeal to some of the darker forces that exist in all our societies  those that can tear us apart ENDEND 
Here in Greece, you’re undergoing similar transformations ENDEND  The first step has been to build a foundation that allows you to return to robust economic growth ENDEND  And we don’t need to recount all the causes of the economic crisis here in Greece ENDEND  If we’re honest, we can acknowledge that it was a mix of both internal and external forces ENDEND  The Greek economy and the level of debt had become unsustainable ENDEND  And in this global economy, investment and jobs flow to countries where governments are efficient, not bloated, where the rules are clear ENDEND  To stay competitive, to attract investment that creates jobs, Greece had to start a reform process ENDEND 
Of course, the world, I don’t think, fully appreciates the extraordinary pain these reforms have involved, or the tremendous sacrifices that you, the Greek people, have made ENDEND  I’ve been aware of it, and I’ve been proud of all that my Administration has done to try to support Greece in these efforts ENDEND  And part of the purpose of my visit is to highlight for the world the important steps that have been taken here in Greece ENDEND 
Today, the budget is back in surplus ENDEND  Parliament passed reforms to make the economy more competitive ENDEND  Yes, there is still much more work to do ENDEND  I want to commend Prime Minister Tsipras for the very difficult reforms his government is pursuing to put the economy on a firmer footing ENDEND  Now, as Greece works to attract more investment, and to prevent old imbalances from reemerging, and to put your economy on a stronger foundation, you’ll continue to have the full support of the United States ENDEND 
At the same time, I will continue to urge creditors to take the steps needed to put Greece on a path towards sustained economic recovery ENDEND  As Greece continues to implement reforms, the IMF has said that debt relief will be crucial to get Greece back to growth ENDEND  They are right ENDEND  It is important because if reforms here are going to be sustained, people need to see hope, and they need to see progress ENDEND  And the young people who are in attendance here today and all across the country need to know there is a future  there is an education and jobs that are worthy of your incredible potential ENDEND  You don’t have to travel overseas, you can put roots right here in your home, in Greece, and succeed ENDEND 
And I’m confident that if you stay the course, as hard as it has been, Greece will see brighter days ENDEND  Because, in this magnificent hall and center  this symbol of the Greek culture and resilience  we’re reminded that just as your strength and resolve have allowed you to overcome great odds throughout your history, nothing can break the spirit of the Greek people ENDEND  You will overcome this period of challenge just as you have other challenges in the past ENDEND 
So economics is something that will be central to preserving our democracies ENDEND  When our economies don’t work, our democracies become distorted and, in some cases, break down ENDEND  But this brings me to another pressing challenge that our democracies face  how do we ensure that our diverse, multicultural, multiracial, multireligious world and our diverse nations uphold both the rights of individuals and a fundamental civic adherence to a common creed that binds us together ENDEND 
Democracy is simplest where everybody thinks alike, looks alike, eats the same food, worships the same God ENDEND  Democracy becomes more difficult when there are people coming from a variety of backgrounds and trying to live together ENDEND  In our globalized world, with the migration of people and the rapid movement of ideas and cultures and traditions, we see increasingly this blend of forces mixing together in ways that often enrich our societies but also cause tensions ENDEND 
In the Information Age, the unprecedented exchange of information can always accentuate differences, or seem to threaten cherished ways of life ENDEND  It used to be that you might not know how people in another part of your country, or in the cities versus the countryside, were living ENDEND  Now everybody knows how everybody is living, and everybody can feel threatened sometimes if people don’t do things exactly the way they do things ENDEND  And they start asking themselves questions about their own identity ENDEND  And it can create a volatile politics ENDEND 
Faced with this new reality where cultures clash, it's inevitable that some will seek a comfort in nationalism or tribe or ethnicity or sect ENDEND  In countries that are held together by borders that were drawn by colonial powers, including many countries in the Middle East and in Africa, it can be tempting to fall back on perceived safety of enclaves and tribal divisions ENDEND 
In a world of widening inequality, there's a growing suspicion  or even disdain  for elites and institutions that seem remote from the daily lives of ordinary people ENDEND  What an irony it is, at a time when we can reach out to people in the most remote corners of the planet, so many citizens feel disconnected from their own governments ENDEND 
So, just as we have to have an inclusive economic strategy, we have to have an exclusive political and cultural strategy ENDEND  In all of our capitals, we have to keep making government more efficient, more effective in responding to the daily needs to citizens ENDEND  Governing institutions, whether in Athens, Brussels, London, Washington, have to be responsive to the concerns of citizens ENDEND  People have to know that they're being heard ENDEND 
Here in Europe, even with today's challenges, I believe that by virtue of the progress it has delivered over the decades  the stability it has provided, the security it's reinforced  that European integration and the European Union remains one of the great political and economic achievements of human history ENDEND  And today more than ever, the world needs a Europe that is strong and prosperous and democratic ENDEND 
But I think all institutions in Europe have to ask themselves: How can we make sure that people within individual countries feel as if their voices are still being heard, that their identities are being affirmed, that the decisions that are being made that will have a critical impact on their lives are not so remote that they have no ability to impact them?
We have to make clear that governments exist to serve the interest of citizens, and not the other way around ENDEND  And so this is why, as President of the United States, I've pursued initiatives like the Open Government Partnership that promotes transparency and accountability so that ordinary people know more about the decisions that affect their lives ENDEND  That's why both at home and around the world, we have taken steps to fight corruption that can rot a society from within ENDEND 
As authoritarian governments work to close space that citizens depend upon to organize and have their voices heard, we've begun the work of empowering civil society to defend democratic values and promote solutions to the problems within our communities ENDEND  And as so many people around the world sometimes are tempted by cynicism and not being involved because they think that politicians and government don’t care about them, we've created networks for young leaders and invested in young entrepreneurs, because we believe that the hope and renewal of our societies begins with the voices of youth ENDEND 
In closing, our globalized world is passing through a time of profound change ENDEND  Yes, there is uncertainty and there is unease, and none of us can know the future ENDEND  History does not move in a straight line ENDEND  Civil rights in America did not move in a straight line ENDEND  Democracy in Greece did not move in a straight line ENDEND  The evolution of a unified Europe certainly has not moved in a straight line ENDEND  And progress is never a guarantee ENDEND  Progress has to be earned by every generation ENDEND  But I believe history gives us hope ENDEND 
Twentyfive centuries after Athens first pointed the way,  years after the beginning of the great American journey, my faith and my confidence, my certainty in our democratic ideals and universal values remain undiminished ENDEND  I believe more strongly than ever that Dr ENDEND  King was right when he said that, “The arc of the moral universe is long, but it bends towards justice ENDEND ” But it bends towards justice not because it is inevitable, but because we bend it towards justice; not because there are not going to be barriers to achieving justice, but because there will be people, generation after generation, who have the vision and the courage and the will to bend the arc of our lives in the direction of a better future ENDEND 
In the United States, and in every place I have visited these last eight years, I have met citizens, especially young people, who have chosen hope over fear, who believe that they can shape their own destiny, who refuse to accept the world as it is and are determined to remake it as it should be ENDEND  They have inspired me ENDEND 
In every corner of the world, I have met people who, in their daily lives, demonstrate that despite differences of race or religion or creed or color, we have the capacity to see each other in ourselves ENDEND  Like the woman here in Greece who said of the refugees arriving on these shores, "We live under the same sun ENDEND  We fall in love under the same moon ENDEND  We are all human  we have to help these people ENDEND " Women like that give me hope ENDEND 
In all of our communities, in all of our countries, I still believe there's more of what Greeks call philotimo  love and respect and kindness for family and community and country, and a sense that we’re all in this together, with obligations to each other ENDEND  Philotimo  I see it every day  and that gives me hope ENDEND 
Because in the end, it is up to us ENDEND  It's not somebody else's job, it's not somebody else's responsibility, but it's the citizens of our countries and citizens of the world to bend that arc of history towards justice ENDEND 
And that’s what democracy allows us to do ENDEND  That's why the most important office in any country is not President or Prime Minister ENDEND  The most important title is “citizen ENDEND ” And in all of our nations, it will always be our citizens who decide the kind of countries we will be, the ideals that we will reach for, and the values that will define us ENDEND  In this great, imperfect, but necessary system of selfgovernment, power and progress will always come from the demos  from “We, the people ENDEND ” And I'm confident that as long as we are true to that system of selfgovernment, that our futures will be bright ENDEND 
Thank you very much ENDEND  Zito e ellas ENDEND

Question: You're about to embark on your final foreign trip ENDEND  What will you say to other world leaders about your successor?  One of the great things about the United States is that when it comes to world affairs, the President obviously is the leader of the executive branch, the CommanderinChief, the spokesperson for the nation, but the influence and the work that we have is the result not just of the President, it is the result of countless interactions and arrangements and relationships between our military and other militaries, and our diplomats and other diplomats, and intelligence officers and development workers ENDEND  And there's enormous continuity beneath the daytoday news that makes us that indispensable nation when it comes to maintaining order and promoting prosperity around the world ENDEND  That will continue ENDEND

With respect to the Democratic Party, look, as I said in the Rose Garden right after the election, when your team loses, everybody gets deflated and it's hard and it's challenging ENDEND  And so I think it's a healthy thing for the Democratic Party to go through some reflection ENDEND  I think it's important for me not to be bigfooting that conversation ENDEND  I think we want to see new voices and new ideas emerge ENDEND  That's part of the reason why I think term limits are a really useful thing ENDEND

So there are going to be a core set of values that shouldn’t be up for debate, should be our North Star ENDEND  But how we organize politically I think is something that we should spend some time thinking about ENDEND  I believe that we have better ideas ENDEND  But I also believe that good ideas don’t matter if people don’t hear them ENDEND  And one of the issues that Democrats have to be clear on is that, given population distribution across the country, we have to compete everywhere ENDEND  We have to show up everywhere ENDEND  We have to work at a grassroots level  something that's been a running thread in my career ENDEND 
I won Iowa not because the demographics dictated that I would win Iowa, it was because I spent  days going to every small town and fair and fish fry and VFW hall ENDEND  And there were some counties where I might have lost, but maybe I lost by  points instead of  points ENDEND  There are some counties maybe I won that people didn’t expect because people had a chance to see you and listen to you and get a sense of who you stood for and who you were fighting for ENDEND

Question: And how long did it take before you were at ease in the job?
 Well, I didn't really have time to worry about being at ease, because you’ll recall we were losing about , jobs a month ENDEND

 I think the American people recognize that the world has shrunk, that it’s interconnected, that you’re not going to put that genie back in the bottle ENDEND  The American people recognize that their careers, or their kids’ careers are going to have to be more dynamic  that they might not be working at a single plant for  years, but they might have to change careers ENDEND  They might have to get more education ENDEND  They might have to retool or retrain ENDEND

And one of the things I advised him to do was to make sure that before he commits to certain courses of action, he’s really dug in and thought through how various issues play themselves out ENDEND  I’ll use an obvious example where we have a difference, but it will be interesting to see what happens in the coming year, and that's the Affordable Care Act ENDEND 
So obviously this has been the Holy Grail for Republicans over the last six, seven years  was “we've got to kill Obamacare ENDEND ” Now, that has been taken as an article of faith  that this is terrible, it doesn’t work and we have to undo it ENDEND 
But now that Republicans are in charge, they got to take a look and say, let's see, we got  million people who have health insurance who didn’t have it before; health care costs generally have gone up at a significantly slower rate since Obamacare was passed than they did before, which has saved the federal Treasury hundreds of billions of dollars; people who have health insurance are benefitting in all sorts of ways that they may not be aware of  everything from no longer having lifetime limits on the claims that they can make, to seniors getting prescription drug discounts under Medicare, to free mammograms ENDEND 
Now, it's one thing to characterize this thing as not working when it's just an abstraction ENDEND  Now, suddenly, you're in charge and you're going to repeal it ENDEND  Okay, well, what happens to those  million people who have health insurance? Are you going to repeal the provision that ensures that if you do have health insurance on your job, and you lose your job or you change jobs or you start a small business that you're not discriminated against because you've got a preexisting condition? That's really popular ENDEND  How are you going to replace it? Are you going to change the policy that kids can stay on their parents' health insurance plan until they're ? How are you going to approach all these issues?
Now, my view is that if they can come up with something better that actually works, and a year or two after they've replaced the Affordable Care Act with their own plan, that  million people have health insurance, and it's cheaper and better and running smoothly, I'll be the first one to say, that's great  congratulations ENDEND 
If, on the other hand, whatever they're proposing results in millions of people losing coverage, and results in people who already have health insurance losing protections that were contained in the legislation, then we're going to have a problem ENDEND  And I think that's not going to be unique to me; I think the American people will respond that way ENDEND 
So I think on a lot of issues, what you're going to see is now comes the hard part ENDEND  Now is governance ENDEND  We are going to be able to present to the incoming Administration a country that is stronger, a federal government that is working better and more efficiently; a national security apparatus that is both more effective and truer to our values; energy policies that are resulting in not just less pollution, but also more jobs ENDEND 
And I think the Presidentelect, rightly, would expect that he's judged on whether we improve from that baseline and on those metrics, or things get worse ENDEND  And if things get worse, then the American people will figure that out pretty quick ENDEND  And if things get better, then more power to him ENDEND  And I'll be the first to congratulate him ENDEND

Zvia, Yoni, Chemi and generations of the Peres family; President Rivlin; Prime Minister Netanyahu; members of the Israeli government and the Knesset; heads of state and the government and guests from around the world, including President Abbas, whose presence here is a gesture and a reminder of the unfinished business of peace; to the people of Israel: I could not be more honored to be in Jerusalem to say farewell to my friend Shimon Peres, who showed us that justice and hope are at the heart of the Zionist idea ENDEND 
A free life, in a homeland regained ENDEND  A secure life, in a nation that can defend itself, by itself ENDEND  A full life, in friendship with nations who can be counted on as allies, always ENDEND  A bountiful life, driven by simple pleasures of family and by big dreams ENDEND  This was Shimon Peres's life ENDEND  This is the State of Israel ENDEND  This is the story of the Jewish people over the last century, and it was made possible by a founding generation that counts Shimon as one of its own ENDEND 
Shimon once said, “The message of the Jewish people to mankind is that faith and moral vision can triumph over all adversity ENDEND ” For Shimon, that moral vision was rooted in an honest reckoning of the world as it is ENDEND  Born in the shtetl, he said he felt, “surrounded by a sea of thick and threatening forests ENDEND ” When his family got the chance to go to Palestine, his beloved grandfather’s parting words were simple: “Shimon, stay a Jew ENDEND ” Propelled with that faith, he found his home ENDEND  He found his purpose ENDEND  He found his life’s work ENDEND  But he was still a teenager when his grandfather was burned alive by the Nazis in the town where Shimon was born ENDEND  The synagogue in which he prayed became an inferno ENDEND  The railroad tracks that had carried him toward the Promised Land also delivered so many of his people to death camps ENDEND 
And so from an early age, Shimon bore witness to the cruelty that human beings could inflict on each other, the ways that one group of people could dehumanize another; the particular madness of antiSemitism, which has run like a stain through history ENDEND  That understanding of man’s everpresent sinfulness would steel him against hardship and make him vigilant against threats to Jewry around the world ENDEND 
But that understanding would never harden his heart ENDEND  It would never extinguish his faith ENDEND  Instead, it broadened his moral imagination, and gave him the capacity to see all people as deserving of dignity and respect ENDEND  It helped him see not just the world as it is, but the world as it should be ENDEND 
What Shimon did to shape the story of Israel is wellchronicled ENDEND  Starting on the kibbutz he founded with his love Sonya, he began the work of building a model community ENDEND  Ben Gurion called him to serve the Haganah at headquarters to make sure that the Jewish people had the armaments and the organization to secure their freedom ENDEND  After independence, surrounded by enemies who denied Israel’s existence and sought to drive it into the sea, the child who had wanted to be a “poet of stars” became a man who built Israel’s defense industry, who laid the foundation for the formidable armed forces that won Israel’s wars ENDEND  His skill secured Israel’s strategic position ENDEND  His boldness sent Israeli commandos to Entebbe, and rescued Jews from Ethiopia ENDEND  His statesmanship built an unbreakable bond with the United States of America and so many other countries ENDEND 
His contributions didn't end there ENDEND  Shimon also showed what people can do when they harness reason and science to a common cause ENDEND  He understood that a country without many natural resources could more than make up for it with the talents of its people ENDEND  He made hard choices to roll back inflation and climb up from a terrible economic crisis ENDEND  He championed the promise of science and technology to make the desert bloom, and turned this tiny country into a central hub of the digital age, making life better not just for people here, but for people around the world ENDEND 
Indeed, Shimon’s contribution to this nation is so fundamental, so pervasive, that perhaps sometimes they can be overlooked ENDEND  For a younger generation, Shimon was probably remembered more for a peace process that never reached its endpoint ENDEND  They would listen to critics on the left who might argue that Shimon did not fully acknowledge the failings of his nation, or perhaps more numerous critics on the right who argued that he refused to see the true wickedness of the world, and called him naïve ENDEND 
But whatever he shared with his family or his closest friends, to the world he brushed off the critics ENDEND  And I know from my conversations with him that his pursuit of peace was never naïve ENDEND  Every Yom HaShoah, he read the names of the family that he lost ENDEND  As a young man, he had fed his village by working in the fields during the day, but then defending it by carrying a rifle at night ENDEND  He understood, in this wartorn region, where too often Arab youth are taught to hate Israel from an early age  he understood just how hard peace would be ENDEND  I'm sure he was alternatively angry and bemused to hear the same critics, who called him hopelessly naïve, depend on the defense architecture that he himself had helped to build ENDEND 
I don’t believe he was naïve ENDEND  But he understood from hardearned experience that true security comes through making peace with your neighbors ENDEND  “We won them all,” he said of Israel’s wars ENDEND  “But we did not win the greatest victory that we aspired to: release from the need to win victories ENDEND ”
And just as he understood the practical necessity of peace, Shimon believed that Israel’s exceptionalism was rooted not only in fidelity to the Jewish people, but to the moral and ethical vision, the precepts of his Jewish faith ENDEND  “The Jewish people weren’t born to rule another people,” he would say ENDEND  “From the very first day we are against slaves and masters ENDEND ”
Out of the hardships of the diaspora, he found room in his heart for others who suffered ENDEND  He came to hate prejudice with the passion of one who knows how it feels to be its target ENDEND  Even in the face of terrorist attacks, even after repeated disappointments at the negotiation table, he insisted that as human beings, Palestinians must be seen as equal in dignity to Jews, and must therefore be equal in selfdetermination ENDEND  Because of his sense of justice, his analysis of Israel’s security, his understanding of Israel’s meaning, he believed that the Zionist idea would be best protected when Palestinians, too, had a state of their own ENDEND 
Of course, we gather here in the knowledge that Shimon never saw his dream of peace fulfilled ENDEND  The region is going through a chaotic time ENDEND  Threats are ever present ENDEND  And yet, he did not stop dreaming, and he did not stop working ENDEND  By the time that I came to work with Shimon, he was in the twilight of his years  although he might not admit it ENDEND  I would be the th United States   President since John F ENDEND  Kennedy to sit down with Shimon; the th to fall prey to his charms ENDEND 
I think of him sitting in the Oval Office, this final member of Israel’s founding generation, under the portrait of George Washington, telling me stories from the past, but more often talking with enthusiasm of the present  his most recent lecture, his next project, his plans for the future, the wonders of his grandchildren ENDEND 
In many ways, he reminded me of some other giants of the th century that I’ve had the honor to meet  men like Nelson Mandela; women like Her Majesty, Queen Elizabeth  leaders who have seen so much, whose lives span such momentous epochs, that they find no need to posture or traffic in what’s popular in the moment; people who speak with depth and knowledge, not in sound bites ENDEND  They find no interest in polls or fads ENDEND 
And like these leaders, Shimon could be true to his convictions even if they cut against the grain of current opinion ENDEND  He knew, better than the cynic, that if you look out over the arc of history, human beings should be filled not with fear but with hope ENDEND  I'm sure that's why he was so excited about technology  because for him, it symbolized the march of human progress ENDEND  And it’s why he loved so much to talk about young people  because he saw young people unburdened by the prejudices of the past ENDEND  It’s why he believed in miracles  because in Israel, he saw a miracle come true ENDEND 
As Americans and Israelis, we often talk about the unbreakable bonds between our nations ENDEND  And, yes, these bonds encompass common interests  vital cooperation that makes both our nations more secure ENDEND  But today we are reminded that the bonds which matter most run deeper ENDEND  Anchored in a JudeoChristian tradition, we believe in the irreducible value of every human being ENDEND  Our nations were built on that idea ENDEND  They were built in large part by stubborn idealists and striving immigrants, including those who had fled war and fled oppression ENDEND  Both our nations have flaws that we have not always fixed, corners of our history which date back to our founding that we do not always squarely address ENDEND  But because our founders planted not just flags in the eternal soil, but also planted the seeds of democracy, we have the ability to always pursue a better world ENDEND  We have the capacity to do what is right ENDEND 
As an American, as a Christian, a person partly of African descent, born in Hawaii  a place that could not be further than where Shimon spent his youth  I took great pleasure in my friendship with this older, wiser man ENDEND  We shared a love of words and books and history ENDEND  And perhaps, like most politicians, we shared too great a joy in hearing ourselves talk ENDEND  But beyond that, I think our friendship was rooted in the fact that I could somehow see myself in his story, and maybe he could see himself in mine ENDEND  Because for all of our differences, both of us had lived such unlikely lives ENDEND  It was so surprising to see the two of us where we had started, talking together in the White House, meeting here in Israel ENDEND  And I think both of us understood that we were here only because in some way we reflected the magnificent story of our nations ENDEND 
Shimon’s story, the story of Israel, the experience of the Jewish people, I believe it is universal ENDEND  It’s the story of a people who, over so many centuries in the wilderness, never gave up on that basic human longing to return home ENDEND  It’s the story of a people who suffered the boot of oppression and the shutting of the gas chamber’s door, and yet never gave up on a belief in goodness ENDEND  And it’s the story of a man who was counted on, and then often counted out, again and again, and who never lost hope ENDEND 
Shimon Peres reminds us that the State of Israel, like the United States of America, was not built by cynics ENDEND  We exist because people before us refused to be constrained by the past or the difficulties of the present ENDEND  And Shimon Peres was never cynical ENDEND  It is that faith, that optimism, that belief  even when all the evidence is to the contrary  that tomorrow can be better, that makes us not just honor Shimon Peres, but love him ENDEND 
The last of the founding generation is now gone ENDEND  Shimon accomplished enough things in his life for a thousand men ENDEND  But he understood that it is better to live to the very end of his time on Earth with a longing not for the past but for the dreams that have not yet come true  an Israel that is secure in a just and lasting peace with its neighbors ENDEND  And so now this work is in the hand of Israel’s next generation, in the hands of Israel's next generation and its friends ENDEND 
Like Joshua, we feel the weight of responsibility that Shimon seemed to wear so lightly ENDEND  But we draw strength from his example and the fact that he believed in us  even when we doubted ourselves ENDEND 
Scripture tells us that before his death, Moses said:
I call upon heaven and earth to bear witness this day that I have set before you life and death, blessing and curse; therefore choose life, that you and your offspring may live ENDEND 
Uvacharta Bachayim ENDEND  Choose life ENDEND  For Shimon, let us choose life, as he always did ENDEND  Let us make his work our own ENDEND  May God bless his memory ENDEND  And may God bless this country, and this world, that he loved so dearly ENDEND
